		<date>2010-02-16</date>
		<username>Robert_Lovelace</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Good Fun, But Marred by a Badly-Paced First Act.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>"The Wolfman" is a remake of the 1941 Universal monster movie; this remake, which has Benicio del Toro as Lawrence Talbot, holds the tradition of classic creature features with an updated use of opportunity and technology that 1941 didn't offer. Del Toro stars as our protagonist, Lawrence, an England-born American playwright and actor who returns to his decrepit family manor in the late 1800s after his brother, Ben, disappears and is found murdered. His father, Sir John Talbot (played by Anthony Hopkins), lurks around the dark manor by himself, while the townspeople whisper of a ravaging beast and/or madman who may be responsible for Ben's demise and countless others. Soon enough, Lawrence has an encounter with the wild beast, which nearly mortally wounds him. In the process, he contracts a curse which turns him from man to beast by the light of a full moon. With the compassion and help provided by Ben's widow, Gwen (played by Emily Blunt), Lawrence attempts to halt the curse before he wreaks unwilling havoc on innocent people.When you go into a film like this, you have to suspend your disbelief and go with the story. It's a dark fairy tale of sorts, laced with curses and lycanthropy that derives from ancient folklore - of course it's not real... but what if it was? That aside, this is a pretty entertaining film, aside from some faults. Joe Johsnton, who brought us action-fare such as "Jumanji" and "Jurassic Park III", directs the film, and provides some good things and some bad things. The film succeeds in thrilling the audience, though I'd be troubled to call it flat-out scary; thrilling is a better word. The CGI-transformation effects are surprisingly convincing, and not too rambunctious (though the fiery finale at the end of the film was ALMOST too much for my tastes). The atmosphere is well-established as well, with the endless Gothic architecture, foggy hollows, and dead leaves; classic set-dressings, ala Tim Burton's "Sleepy Hollow" and other films of the sort. But it's nicely done, and it works.Now, the single most problem this film suffers from is pacing. The pacing in the first act of the film is, dare I say it: awful. I felt like I was watching an action movie with werewolves, not a werewolf movie with action; and it's not even because there was too much action going on, no - it was because things were simply happening too fast. There was no establishment of characters to speak of, and everything felt amped-up for no reason. The first thirty-forty minutes of the film seemed like a stream of badly-transitioned scenes that are overplayed with dramatic music to make things tense; the problem with this is that we are never given any downtime to begin with. There are no silences, no build-ups; it's just bam, bam, bam- one scene after another. That's fine for a finale when the action is rising, but for an introduction/exposition? That's just messy.Aside from that, I can't complain too much about any other facets of the film. The acting is wonderful. I expected good performances from Del Toro and Hopkins (and they are both very good), but Emily Blunt is the one who shines in this; she's certainly proved herself enough as a serious actress, and she does great work, even as a damsel-in-distress clad in lovely Victorian gowns and Edwardian hats. Hugo Weaving also has a large part as a detective, and he's great as well. The film's finale is good, even if the fiery fight is a bit too "action movie" for my tastes. The final scene at the waterfall, though, is very well-played. As for the best wolf scene, I'd definitely have to say that the asylum observatory scene is the very best, along with the subsequent reign of terror that Benicio brings throughout downtown London. Fun stuff, though I have to say, the gypsies were a bit under-used.Overall, "The Wolfman" justifies itself enough as a solid remake of a classic monster movie. Creature features aren't really my thing, but I enjoyed this one because of its setting and acting. It's very well-photographed and has enough thrills and monster violence to please horror fans, and enough action to keep adrenaline junkies awake. However, the film's fatal flaw is its initial pacing structure, which brings things down a bit. As it progresses, this improves, but the first forty minutes are poorly laced together. Even still, I can't say it wasn't fun. 7/10.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Finally a Man that's a Wolf</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>As a fan of 1941's Wolf Man, Wolf, Ginger Snaps films and (my #1 all time pick) The Howling (ORIGINAL only) I am very, very sad there's only a handful of good werewolf movies out there.I know it's an unpopular opinion, and that never bothers me, but I either strongly disliked or hated the following werewolf attempts: any/all of the Howling sequels, the Underworld films, Cursed, Van Helsing, both An American Werewolf in London and Paris, Silver Bullet, and Bad Moon, just to name a few. Also, I cannot stand to see a human morphing into a dog (they call it a wolf, I say dog because it looks like it and it's absolutely no "Wolf-MAN") with one exception: Wolf. Otherwise, that's not scary to me and it doesn't look like a man that turned into a wolf ? it just looks like a nature show. Since the great majority of the so-called "werewolf" movies, Twilight: New Moon, Wolfen, An American Werewolf in London, etc, has a man turning into a dog, I simply look elsewhere. And finally, CGI has NOT been a blessing for one of my favorite movie monsters. Underworld not only did it almost 100% CGI, they decided, rightfully so, that it still looked fake. So what did they do? Sped up the action so fast, that you have barely 3 seconds in the entire movie to see any sort of werewolf. When you get "privileged" with those 3 seconds, you're disappointed in how fake and funny they look. On the flipside, Van Helsing also used CGI but slowed it down. Unfortunately for them, it was so hilariously bad looking, it seemed like everyone was afraid of a big teddy bear.So, it was refreshing to see Hollywood get back on track with an actual WOLF-MAN in 2010's latest remake of Wolf Man. I'm sure they used CGI, but it looks more like to me like they simply did old-school make up (and in my opinion the ONLY way to do this kind of creature. Sorry CGI buffs and people in love with the blue people of Avatar. Cameron might think this is the future, but I feel strongly CGI is beginning of the end.) While in some scenes, the werewolf is fast and vicious, in most he's (or they're) just standing there. Finally, we get to see a real-looking werewolf.In addition, the sets, the cinematography, the feel, the look of the late 19th Century England was beautiful. At first, I was opposed to making this film as a period piece, but the end result made it more creepy and more of a genuine remake of the 1941 classic vs. just another "modern" film.That all said, the rest of the movie was, well, just okay. There were far too many attempts at frights, most of which were so far in advance set up, you just sat and waited for them. A great majority didn't work, though admittedly one or two did make me jump. But that resulted in approximately 1-2% of the entire movie. This movie should frighten probably anyone who gets scared at simple stuff, like Paranormal Activity or any of the 1930-1940s original monster movies. Or any young kids.This brings me to another point: this is NOT for kids. Or even most teens. It might have been suitable for even younger teens, but they poured on the gore. This is NOT your grandfather's wolf-man. This is the Saw-Gen-Wolfman. Now, I didn't really have a problem at all with this. At least they had the balls enough to make an adult version of the classic, vs. making the idiotic PG-13 cut to make more money at the box office.Lawrence Talbot returns from USA to England when the search for his missing brother is just winding down. It turns out the entire town not only believed they're cursed with a man-beast on the loose, but fear that after a soon-to-be attack on a gypsy village and a bite on Talbot's neck, he'll be stalking/killing them too.Keeping in tradition, he can only change during a full moon, and the townie's attempts to thwart another series of murders by capturing/killing Talbot are moot as father Talbot protects his remaining child. There's some mystery on the other wolf-man on the loose and that's seen a mile away. When that's finally revealed, the scenes lead up to the eventual battle of the beasts.Throw in a fantastic London transformation scene and rooftop chase and a so-called love story from Talbot's sister-in-law and you have the entire movie.What worked: Great sets and feel. Wonderful transformation scenes. Great viewings of the werewolves. Old school techniques used right.What didn't work, unfortunately, is a longer list starting with: bad to mediocre acting. Both Hopkins and Del Toro all but looked bored and never before had I seen a tremendously bland job by either great actor. (Weaving just played/copied his "Agent Smith" role from Matrix.) Since they kept to "the full moon" predicament, they limited the number of wolf-man changes. The problem arises with believability in time frames, i.e. he's running from London back home and when he shows up, another full month had passed. Really? Also, predictable scenes, dialogue and twists. A "love" story that fell flat and was completely unconvincing. It was a tad bit slow at times. It suffered a somewhat confusing script when it could've been more straightforward. In addition, the dialogue was poor, bland and not one line stood out.This movie is for die-hard werewolf fans only. It has just enough morphing scenes and full-on wolf-man shots to keep me interested, though I would've preferred at least one more scene. I'm still awarding it 3/5 stars with strong reservations. It's not a horrible movie and it did a great job of giving you that 1940s feel all over again, aside from the gore. Worth a shot.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>THE WOLFMAN (2010) *** Benicio Del Toro, Anthony Hopkins, Emily Blunt, Hugo Weaving, Art Malik, Geraldine Chaplin, Simon Merrells, Cristina Contes. Bloody good (for the most part) remake of the 1941 Lon Chaney, Jr. /Universal Pictures classic about to-the-manor-born Lawrence Talbot (Del Toro, decidedly vulpine to boot and producer of this pet project/labour of love ? and it shows!), whose return to his home to search for his missing brother winds up the victim of his sibling's killer ? a werewolf - which in turn passes the lurid lycanthropic curse onto him. Fiercely interpreted by director Joe Johnston with gangbusters production design by Rick Heinrichs, gorgeously shot cinematography by Shelly Johnson, Danny Elfman's eerie score, and the true star of the film ? Rick Baker (self-professed "Monster Maker" and who by the way has a dilly of a cameo as one of the Gypsy camps' attack first victim! ? for incorporating some of Jack Pierce's designs from back in the day and employing his own crafty excellent creature interpretations that are truly scary and hairy (pun intended); all make up for the rather bare-bones yet true to the source material screenplay by Andrew Kevin Walker &amp; David Self. Gory to a fault and Del Toro has a field day; will have you howling for more!</fullReview>

		<date>2011-07-06</date>
		<username>Spikeopath</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Extended Cut: Proving in fact that The Wolfman is no monstrosity.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Wolfman is directed by Joe Johnston and adapted to screenplay by Andrew Kevin Walker and David Self from Curt Siodmak's original 1941 screenplay. It stars Benicio del Toro, Anthony Hopkins, Emily Blunt and Hugo Weaving. Music is scored by Danny Elfman and Shelly Johnson is the cinematographer. Plot is set in 1891 and finds Toro as Lawrence Talbot, who upon learning of his brother's grizzly murder, returns to his estranged father's estate in Blackmoor to investigate. Upon arriving he forms an instant bond with his dead sibling's fiancée, Gwen (Blunt), but soon Lawrence finds himself mired in a world of lycanthropy and family secrets.It had a much publicised troubled production, with a director switch, numerous release date changes and enforced cuts to the running time, sitting atop of this particular iceberg. With that it mind, film is arguably better than it had any right to be. What is now readily available is an extended cut of the film where some 17 minutes of footage have been put back into the story. Footage cut originally to foolishly speed up the moment when the first transformation from man to wolfman arrives. Is the extended cut a far better movie than the trimmed theatrical version? The answer is resounding yes it is. It still contains flaws, but now the narrative is stronger and crucially by waiting much longer for the first "big change scene", tension and anticipation now exist where previously it didn't. In spite of some modern day advancements for this 21st Century wolfman-excessive speed for the wolf, creature smack down finale-Johnston's movie is very faithful and respectful of the 1941 original. Which is perhaps not surprising since the weight of Universal is backing this remake of one of their original classic monsters from the 30's and 40's. But it's nice to see that a remake keeps the things that made the original so beloved. Gothic texture is rich, none more so than with the mist/smog shrouded village, while the tortured sadness that permeated Lon Chaney's take on Larry Talbot back in 1941, is perfectly essayed by del Toro this time around. While the creature design itself cleaves close to the original, with Rick Baker and Dave Elsey picking up the Academy Award for Best Makeup for their excellent efforts. Fans of shred and gore are also well served here, with Johnston (The Rocketeer/Jumanji) showing a keen eye for action construction. An attack at a Gypsy camp and a bloody rampage through a bus are the stand outs, while an asylum sequence is also not to be sniffed at. Chuck in the murder mystery element, and the intriguing father and son axis, and it's a film that has more to it than merely being a CGI popcorn piece. Even Elfman's score fits neatly into the period setting. Problem wise it comes down to Blunt and Weaving being under used, and Hopkins veering dangerously close to over hamming the omelette, yet all remain strong in characterisations. Toro, though, is not, as some have suggested, miscast. Already lupine like before transformation, he plays it perfectly troubled and brooding like. Talbot has inner demons and unanswered questions in his past, a past he has tried to shred but finds he now must confront those demons. The onset of lycanthropy serving, one feels, as a metaphor for his personal torment finally being unleashed. Toro nails it with an excellent, subdued, portrayal; though choice of hair style leaves much to be desired. Worth a mention as well is Shelly Johnson's photography, where nice atmospheric visuals complement the tone and period flavour of the piece. More homage than outright horror, but with a little something for everyone interested in Horror/Gothic/Mystery type movies, The Wolfman in extended form is an enjoyable enough experience. 7/10</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2010-03-01</date>
		<username>MerryMarvelManiac</username>
		<score>0.9</score>
		<shortDescription>The Wolfman: Triumphant return of the legendary monster!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Going into this film, I had serious reservations, mostly due to the well publicized production troubles that seemed to plague it from the start. Having seen it three times now, I can tell you that any criticisms are undeserved. This film is a serious monster masterpiece. The settings and production design are top notch, as is the incredible updated Wolfman makeup by master Rick Baker. The cast all do an admirable job of maintaining the feel of a classic horror film throughout. Benicio Del Toro is exceptional as Lawrence Talbot, the doomed Wolfman, and at times seems to be channeling Lon Chaney Jr. through his performance. It really is uncanny. Anthony Hopkins and Hugo Weaving make for interesting characters who each move the story along in their own inimitable way, and Emily Blunt is wonderfully cast as the perplexed Gwen Conliffe. I found Joe Johnston's directorial style to be exhilarating. He doesn't waste time going over old territory we're already familiar with, and instead moves the story along with such force that you feel helplessly caught up in this tragic chain of events, just like the participants. Every major event in the film coincides with a full moon, with scarcely any time in between. This further amplifies the feeling of dread, as the viewer is never given a chance to "relax" during the 28 days until the next appearance of the beast. The month flies by, as I'm sure it would for the afflicted werewolf. Some really nice nods to the monster movies of old include terrified villagers scrambling to bolt their doors and windows as the Wolfman's hideous howl fills the night, and the creaky, fog-shrouded forest of Blackmoor, it's trees black and twisted under the silvery light of the full moon. Topping all this off is an exceptionally creepy score by Danny Elfman, with a distinct European and Gypsy flavor. This is a great movie, full of scares, and a fitting tribute to those Universal masterpieces of the 1930's and 40's. So what are you waiting for? Dim the lights, grab some popcorn, and if it's a full moon...well, you might want to bring along that silver-handled cane...just in case!</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>JoeKarlosi</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>The Wolfman (2010) ***</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>A reworking of the 1941 horror classic, about a young man who returns home after his brother's death, encounters a werewolf, and becomes infected by its bite with the curse which causes him to transform into a man-beast during the cycle of the full moon. I felt this was a fairly good horror film in its own different way ... and I'd easily consider it the best of all those "modernized werewolf movies" of the last 30 years. This one blows the overrated "An American Werewolf In London" and "The Howling" comedies out of the woods. It's also miles ahead of Jack Nicholson's pedestrian "Wolf".First, I loved the Victorian atmosphere and the cast. I thought Del Toro, Hopkins, and Blunt were top-notch; ditto for the other supporting characters. The Wolfman makeup (by monster movie fan Rick Baker) is superb -- he is fierce and deadly, and SCARY... I jumped a few times in my seat, and that's not something that happens very often with me and new horror movies. I appreciated that the subject matter was handled seriously and straight - no laughs. I liked how there were elements of both Universal and Hammer in there, sort of like the way the Hammer MUMMY managed to include elements of all the Universal Kharis films. I was able to feel some sympathy for Lawrence Talbot even though he was portrayed as a rather gloomy soul right from the get-go ... when we see how he's mistreated by a mental hospital, and how his father feels about him as the movie advances. I felt the bond between Larry and Gwen Conliffe, who becomes his heartbroken love interest, was pretty strong. I thought the gore effects delivered, so there's also that " '80s element " tossed in as well for that particular audience.Is it a PERFECT movie? No. A GREAT film? No way. But it delivers the goods for horror movie lovers, and I think that's what we here care about and why so many of us seem to like it. I was relieved that, while there is noticeable and needless CGI in places, it did not get overused to the point where it ruined the movie (as was the case with the 1999 Sommers MUMMY). I didn't need the werewolf galloping on all fours, but it didn't go overboard in that feature and at times it even worked. I also thought there seemed to be some editing questions here and there -- as if sometimes we jumped to something else and missed some key information (Gwen going to her antiques shoppe, seemingly out of nowhere and with no explanation of where it was or that it even existed?). Maybe I missed something there. At first I was a bit brought down by a "reimagining of Sir John" angle and I thought it hurt the movie ... but I was able to turn it around and by the time the reality sank in, it started to work for me as part of the story. I really didn't like the "set up" ending; that really got in the way and just was not required.So .... overall, a very pleasant surprise, and a remake that can stand alongside the 1941 classic (which in itself was flawed and not perfect) in its own right. I personally can't see why it's getting such so-so reviews from mainstream critics. It's a horror movie, for cryin' out loud - and an effective one at that. *** out of ****</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Brian W. Fairbanks (brianwfairbanks@yahoo.com)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Not great, but not quite a disaster either</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The remake of The Wolfman does not resonate the way the original 1941 film does. I even found myself thinking back to scenes from that version while watching this ultimately disappointing revision. In a sense, it's inaccurate to say I was disappointed because I really wasn't expecting much. Even before I read the mostly dismissive reviews, the film's history had marked it as a misfire. In addition to several changes in directors, the film was scheduled for release in spring 2009, then rescheduled several times before finally opening this weekend. But though this is no classic, or even a movie that's likely to linger in the mind for more than a minute after the credits roll, it's hardly a disaster. It certainly helps to have a fondness for the Universal horror classics from the '30s and '40s, even though this film has more in common with the lesser films from that period. It's more on a par with House of Frankenstein or The Mummy's Ghost than with Dracula, Bride of Frankenstein, or The Black Cat. On the plus side, the sets are impressive, and the effects are effective, but not surprisingly, the emphasis is on gore more than atmosphere. It lacks the haunting power of the original which was simpler, less pretentious, and superior despite special effects that were limited to fog and Jack Pierce's makeup for Lon Chaney, Jr. (Universal dropped the "Jr" in the official credits.)Blood and guts are very prominent in this full color remake which draws on but wildly diverts from Curt Siodmak's 1941 screenplay, and also pays slight homage to Hammer's Curse of the Werewolf with Oliver Reed. As is typical of contemporary horror films (and those based on comic book characters), everything is overdone, including the story which presents us with two werewolves. The climax is a battle between the two snarling creatures that reminded me of the fight between the two beasts in the Edward Norton version of The Incredible Hulk. The best scene is one in an asylum where Larry Talbot, believed to be insane, is gagged and bound in a roomful of doctors while a full moon is on the rise outside. Their theories about Talbot's mental state are soon ripped to bloody shreds.Benicio Del Toro is fine as Talbot, Emily Blunt is lovely as his late brother's fiancée, and Anthony Hopkins is okay, but he's on hand to lend the film his stature more than his talent. Rick Baker's makeup is effective, and Danny Elfman's score is okay, but not as memorable as Frank Skinner's simpler, yet spookier, score for the original. The Wolfman is far from great, and it leaves no cliché untouched, but it delivers enough of the ingredients you'd expect from a werewolf movie to earn a passing grade. Brian W. Fairbanks</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>super_wr_83</username>
		<score>0.2</score>
		<shortDescription>Wolfman a Howler</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Way back in 1941, George Waggner created a horror staple in The Wolf Man. We haven't all seen it, but it's regarded as one of the very first horror masterpieces. Fast forward to 2010, the decade of gritty reboots. Joe Johnston has created a laugher in The Wolfman. The story revolves around Lawrence Talbot (Benicio Del Toro, Snatch) a renowned Shakespearean actor come home to England from America after the mysterious and gruesome death of his brother Ben. His father, Sir John Talbot (Anthony Hopkins, Silence of the Lambs), seems oddly unaffected by his son's horrific death. Ben's fiancé, Gwen Conliffe (Emily Blunt, The Devil Wears Prada), tries to leave the Talbot's manor in an attempt to escape the same terrible fate as her fiancé, but is inevitably dragged back because... of something. I don't know.Let's start with the good. Del Toro and Hopkins are fantastic as always. They capture their characters in the same effortless way as they have in all their previous endeavors. Blunt is also quite good in her portrayal. They had several homages back to the original. For example, Sir John Talbot is the actor who originally played the Wolf Man. Now that we've covered that, let's go over the bad. My biggest problem was the writing. The dialog was clunky at best, nauseatingly melodramatic at worst. The plot (if it can be called that) was poorly defined at times, and completely ignored most of the time. Part of what defines the original Wolf Man as such a classic is the costuming and make-up. Today, with such films as District 9 or more pointedly, Avatar, the computer imaging is expected to be as close to perfect as possible. In Wolfman, the imagery is downright terrible. When the werewolves go into their four-legged run, they look less like an animal than they do a poorly constructed animatronic at Disneyland. They use computer generation at completely unnecessary times as well. Call me a purist, but a horse is not that difficult to ride. There is absolutely no reason that you can't at least put a stunt rider on the animal if your star has broken his tail bone or something.This is supposed to be a horror movie. Audiences today aren't impressed with the monster appearing loudly and suddenly and startling them. Audiences want Paranormal Activity, where the director subtly uses effects, small noises, and dialog to honestly scare them. The werewolf and a feral child that resembles Gollum from The Lord of the Rings closely enough to be considered copyright infringement pop out of nowhere and disappear suddenly enough times and in such a similar manner that they probably could have just reused the same shots from earlier in the film. That is, if the editor could get out of his own way.Speaking of the editing, some cuts are downright terrible, with the characters completely changing positions, holding new items, or the background being drastically altered. Admittedly, there are directors who do this ironically, who edit in ways that make cigarettes grow, then shrink, then grow again, but that was clearly not the intention in this movie.When it comes right down to it, this movie is awful. The audience at the midnight screening I attended was laughing, and it wasn't because of the witty, fresh dialog. It's in that horrible wasteland that some movies inhabit where it's not nearly bad enough to be a farce, but not nearly good enough to be worth spending money on. It's not nearly good enough to spend time on. I beg you, dear reader, save yourself the pain and save Benicio Del Toro and Anthony Hopkins the embarrassment and stay home and watch some Hannibal movies and maybe Fear and Loathing in Las Vegas. Then watch 1941's The Wolf Man. Don't see this movie.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<date>2014-03-21</date>
		<username>loomis78-815-989034</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Very Bloody, but where is the tragedy?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Almost all of the classic Universal Studios monster movies have been remade, some of them multiple times through the years. Two of the best known; "Creature from the Black Lagoon" and "The Wolfman" had not been remade until now. The creature will continue to wait for its redo, but The Wolfman's time has come. The 1941 classic with Lon Chaney JR. could be one of Universals most tragic monsters of all time. This remake is reborn in the capable hands of Benicio Del Toro in the title role as Lawrence Talbot. Set in the year 1891, Talbot (Del Toro) is an actor who was working in America and is called back to his home in England with the discovery of the brutal death of his brother Ben. He reunites with his father John (Hopkins) and brother's fiancé Gwen (Blunt) who is staying at the crumbling family mansion. Hearing that Gypsies may have had something to do with his brother's demise, Lawrence goes to their camp in hope of answers. Out of nowhere, a vicious beast attacks in bloody fashion. Talbot is bitten by it while trying to rescue a boy in the woods. When the next full moon rises, Talbot transforms into the Wolfman and goes on a rampage. The local Scotland Yard inspector Abberline (Weaving) captures Talbot who is rushed off and placed into an asylum. It is obvious that father John knows more than he is telling. Meanwhile, at the asylum a foolish doctor proceeds to present Talbot's condition as only being in his head before a medical theater. Talbot explodes into the Wolfman and more bloody mayhem ensues as he impales the doctor and runs through the city slicing up the town people with Abberline in pursuit. Once Lawrence comes out of it he returns home to confront his father about the secret he knows and what really happened to his mother. This much delayed remake kept getting pulled back by Universal. It's not clear what the problems were and why it kept being pulled back but the story comes across as choppy and fragmented. It feels like you are watching the chunks that were left after the meat of the story had been cut out. This remake wanders from the original films storyline mainly concerning the true identity of father John. This comes in the way of a late in the film story twist that leaves a lot to be desired. This twist was truly unnecessary and does not help the film much at all. Del Toro is competent as Talbot, but the ragged story doesn't give him much to do but wander from scene to scene wondering what he did when he was the Wolfman. Chaney JR.'s emotional depth of his character is what made that version so tragic. This remake ignores the desperate anguish that the original had and seems only interested in Talbot acting on revenge against those who wronged him. The cast is solid all the way around with Hugo weaving stealing scenes as the Inspector. Director Joe Johnston makes the film look fantastic. The full moon and its effect on atmosphere and mood really help. The time period it is set in also makes the film seem authentic. Rick Baker's use of practical effects mixed with small doses of CGI really worked making the transformation scenes great. The Wolfman never looked better. This film gets bloody, really bloody. Gore hounds will enjoy the splatter of blood everywhere. Intestines are ripped out; heads and limbs get knocked off bodies' right and left. The rampage through town is especially gory. There is something to be said for less is more. There is no doubt if this were real the trail of bodies and limbs would be like depicted here. As a film the excessive gore doesn't really match the tone and setting, and it just might have been scarier if more was left to the audiences mind. Johnston gets some good scares and even some jumps out of his audience by making this vicious beast act like this. The ferocious attacks by the beast are intense and catch the viewer off guard, which works to good effect. Gone is the subtlety and tragic feel of the original and what is left is a brutal and bloody remake.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>To start, i am a huge fan of Benicio del Toro so whenever i see a movie of his i am a little biased in how i view the movie, for the positive of course. His performance in this film was deeply nuanced and something that i did not expect for him to pull off quite so well, but keeping that in mind i felt like the film didn't leave much for del Toro to work with. Now first, this film did win the academy award for make-up because it was really good, set design was good, the costumes were good, but where it shined in aesthetics it lacked in writing quality. This film had a stellar cast, Benicio del Toro, Anthony Hopkins, Hugo Weaving, Emily Blunt all of whom i think are great actors but i'm afraid the writing didn't leave them much to work with. It felt like i was watching another rendition of Stephen Sommers' Van Helsing only with better actors and make-up.Don't get me wrong it wasn't terrible, it just felt like a high school play with a multi million dollar budget. If you're going to get those names and spend that kind of money on special effects the film should at least be interesting, at least beyond the central plot of the film, which neither of the two writers who created this adaption of the 1941 original came up with. Any addition made to the main story by these writers was a needless one and it did not contribute to the plot or character development any. In essence it really felt like two guys in their basement decided that they loved the original Wolfman so much that they wanted to do their own version with their own plot twists, but instead of them using a super 8 camera they used state of the art high tech digital cameras and tons of CGI to capture their less than interesting visions. Now granted i'm not a huge fan of films in this genre, but i know when i see a good one and i don't think this film was it. This film did not capture my imagination like i wanted it to and i didn't feel connected with the characters, the only thing that kept me watching was that i wanted to see what would happen at the end, even though in the back of my mind i knew what the ending was without ever seeing the original film, the plot was just that predictable and unoriginal. So overall, a decently made film but nothing amazing, if your looking for a movie you don't have to think about this is great otherwise don't waste your time.</fullReview>

		<date>2012-12-04</date>
		<username>ShootingShark</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Lavish, Atmospheric, Quality Remake Of Classic Universal Monster Movie</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>When his brother dies in gruesome circumstances, thespian Lawrence Talbot returns to his ancestral home to investigate. One night he visits a nearby gypsy camp amid rumours of a strange beast on the loose, but tragedy awaits ...This enjoyable and scary reworking of the classic 1941 George Waggner / Lon Chaney Jr film has most everything a good remake should - a bigger budget to do the story real justice, many references to the original whilst creating its own visual style, a top-notch cast, and a storyline which adds many interesting twists to the tale. I also get the sense that all the filmmakers wanted to make a proper horror film, which is played straight as pure tragedy, set in 1891, intensely dark and almost old-fashioned in its sensibilities. It's a movie about a cursed family, blood, the moon and horror, and that's pretty much it. Like the 1979 Hollywood remake of Dracula, it's a powerful colour retelling of a great old scary story. I've always loved werewolf movies, and they set a unique challenge for the actor playing the monster; they must be sympathetic, but at the same time a murderous beast - how do you do this ? Del Toro is great; quiet and thoughtful, overcome with suffering and grief, his performance juxtaposes well with Hopkins' grandiose firebrand as he quotes the Gospel of Luke and generally causes mayhem, whilst the strong supporting cast all acquit themselves well. Best of all however is the ever-reliable Weaving, as the dour-faced Inspector Abberline (the real life detective from the Jack the Ripper case), who steals all his scenes - don't miss his perfectly delivered soliloquy to the pub landlady. The visual scope of the film is fantastic - the sets and costumes are excellent, Danny Elfman's full-on orchestral score is wonderful and transports the listener to the amazing other world depicted, and the second half particularly is full of wild scenes as the monster rampages across the moonlit rooftops of Victorian London. I also really love the special effects by Rick Baker, who goes for a classic hairy biped creature - this movie is about a Wolf Man - which not only looks sensational but is a loving tribute to its predecessor. There are a few things I don't like, chief amongst them being the pacing, which is too fast, rushing through flashbacks and dreams instead of taking time to build suspense, and the development of the Talbot family's fortune (particularly the Hopkins character) takes some swallowing. Director Johnston (Honey I Shrunk The Kids, Jumanji) shows a sure hand however and blends all the elements of the film together well, creating a modern Gothic blockbuster with thrills and chills aplenty. A great movie and a must for all fans of Cinema Lycanthropa.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Turfseer</username>
		<score>0.4</score>
		<shortDescription>Wonderful set design and musical score can't save pointless blood-fest!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>'The Wolfman' is a remake of the 1941 horror 'classic' of the same name, starring Lon Chaney Jr. By changing the setting to Victorian England, the remake is light years ahead of the original in the set design department. I happened to love all the costumes, the locales where the film was shot as well as the Danny Elfman score which manages to add a lot more weight to the film, which suffers from the original paltry concept and some noxious re-imaginings found in this new revision.I simply fail to understand why people are drawn to the whole Wolfman concept in the first place. It has little basis in reality unless you're drawn to those stories of humans raised by apes in the jungle who are unable to throw off their primitive inclinations due to their unusual upbringing. Of course in those rare cases, the human being doesn't find himself transformed into a beast and then reverting to human form, whenever daylight finally breaks.As a myth, the Wolfman seems awfully simplistic. What does one learn after undergoing the 'Wolfman' experience in the movies? That men are capable of horrible violence when the thin veneer of civilization is swept away? So what! In a sense, the Wolfman character is a paganistic, pre-Christian representative of the Devil. Where the Devil is a beast in human form, the Wolfman is a human in a fake, beast-like form. Those viewers with unconscious aggressive wishes are vicariously drawn in part to the fake beast, who with his superhuman strength, rips peoples' heads off with a flick of his paw-like hand. Not only do they take delight in a character that can best be found conjured up in a five-year-old's nightmare, but also cheer just as loudly, when the fake creature meets its end at the hands of a superstitious, vengeful mob. The mob finally figures out a way to snuff out the life of this maniacal figure, who somehow emanates from a dream-like world. It's much more difficult coming to terms with devils such as the Nazis, whose evil had a human face and were not so easily defeated as the Wolfman is here.'The Wolfman' (the remake) changes the plot of the original 1941 film by introducing the idea that the Wolfman's father, Sir John Talbot, is actually a Wolfman himself and is responsible for the death of his son, Ben Talbot. Lawrence Talbot, now an American actor (having left England after the death of mother), initially returns to the family estate to investigate the disappearance of his brother, but soon learns that the brother has been murdered. Later, it's revealed that Sir John's faithful servant, Singh, has been keeping him locked up for years in the castle whenever the full moon appears, preventing the father from escaping and fomenting havoc throughout the countryside.The problem is that Sir John is an utterly, unlikable character who's depicted as not having one modicum of charm. He's such a churl that he doesn't hesitate in informing Lawrence that he's responsible for murdering his mother, his brother and also turning Lawrence into a werewolf as well. Instead of sticking up for the son (who's being hunted by a determined Scotland Yard Inspector as well as assorted meanies from the town), Sir John would rather (pardon the pun), throw his son to the wolves! Lawrence as the Wolfman has a similar problem. Unlike the Frankenstein monster or a modern version such as Terminator II, he's not what you would call, a 'conflicted' creature. The good side never comes out until maybe the end, when he realizes that it's Gwen (the woman who falls for both Ben and Lawrence), who he's about to rip apart, turning her into the expected umpteenth bloody mess. While Lawrence terrorizes both urban and rural parts of England, he's simply a killing machine, ripping all kinds of body parts from living human beings. All this becomes tiresome since we're praying that perhaps his human side might emerge and the Inspector will now have reasons in hesitating to do the creature in.Was anyone else bothered by the fact that the The Wolfman's demise is a foregone conclusion? I found it deflating that Maleva, the gypsy woman, informs Gwen that there's no cure for the Wolfman's curse. The best we can look forward to is that the Wolfman dies in the presence of someone who loves him. Once all that is brought out, the climax is utterly predictable and one realizes that 'The Wolfman' is a film with virtually no surprises.'The Wolfman' could have been a much more interesting film if it had some stakes (pardon the pun again), that you could bite your teeth into! How about a satire or critique on the Victorian age--the era in which the film is set in? In the end, 'The Wolfman' is all atmosphere. One must be content with admiring the attention to detail?yes, dig those illustrations on the front page of the newspaper that chronicles "the monster's" deeds, that the Inspector thrusts in Gwen's face when he visits her antique store! Very cool! As for the Wolfman story?unfortunately, that's something that's not very hard to forget, in a virtual instant.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Scott LeBrun (Hey_Sweden)</username>
		<score>-1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Almost 70 years later, The Wolfman remains a great tragic character.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Lawrence Talbot (Benicio Del Toro) has been away from home for many years, working as a prominent stage actor, after witnessing a traumatic sight as a young boy. Upon learning of the death of his brother, he returns home to his father Sir John (Anthony Hopkins) and his brothers' fiancée Gwen (Emily Blunt). During the hunt for the beast that savaged his brother, he gets badly bitten himself, surviving to carry the curse of lycanthropy.Although apparently respectful of the classic 1941 werewolf movie starring Lon Chaney, Jr., this remake is too much in the mold of some modern horror movies, ultimately abandoning any sense of subtlety in favour of a full steam ahead, action packed approach, with our rampaging monster running and leaping all about the place, video game style. That's not to say that this big budget production doesn't get some things right. For one thing, the exterior and location work is great, with fine work done by production designer Rick Heinrichs and cinematographer Shelly Johnson. Danny Elfmans' music is absolutely thunderous at times. Rick Baker, revered for his memorable effects for "An American Werewolf in London", creates an effective looking creature (itself showing the proper amount of respect for the iconic look that Jack Pierce gave Chaney back in '41) as well as some very juicy gore (in the unrated version). Excellent atmosphere is set up well immediately, but we get a little too good a look at the monster too soon, eliminating some of the suspense.Del Toro, also one of the producers, is very understated as the doomed Lawrence, while the beautiful Blunt is appealing as his concerned leading lady. The evolving relationship between them does give the movie the emotional centre that is needed. Hugo Weaving is amusing as the inspector confronted with this hideous murder spree, and Art Malik is solid as Singh. Geraldine Chaplin is good as Maleva, even though it's inevitable that she won't make anyone forget Maria Ouspenskaya. With Hopkins always playing his character as somewhat "off", the ultimate reveal regarding Sir John shouldn't come as too much of a surprise.One interesting new touch is the inclusion of asylum scenes (Lawrence had already been confined to this place as a child) wherein skeptical doctors are determined to prove that Lawrences' affliction is all in his head; seeing Lawrence subjected to what amounts to torture is very uncomfortable. The ending is just as emotionally resonant as is necessary, and one can feel the characters' pain.This would rate as a good effort overall that at the very least I would not consider boring.Seven out of 10.</fullReview>

		<fullReview>"The Wolfman" Upon learning of his brother's disappearance, Lawrence Talbot (Benicio Del Toro) returns to the home he left many years before. When he arrives, he finds the house in shambles with his father, Sir John Talbot (Anthony Hopkins), teetering on the edge of insanity. When his brother's body is discovered, Lawrence stays around, in part to comfort his brother's former fiancé Gwen (Emily Blunt), and in part to determine how his brother died. The story around town is that the death was at the hands of the Wolfman, a local legend. Soon Lawrence is himself attacked by the creature, setting up a brutal showdown with the demons of his past.At one point, "Wolfman" was so respected as to garner award anticipation. Quite quickly, however, this attention waned as the movie was shifted all over the calendar until it finally settled into the Movie Dead Zone that is February. I held out a small measure of hope if for no other reason than the actors involved. I think Del Toro is an excellent talent who usually delivers and while Hopkins hasn't done anything of value in years (and years and years), I still want to believe he's got something left in the tank.Unfortunately, "Wolfman" is a train wreck. The story is mediocre at best and seriously choppy throughout. There is little to no character development and the special effects are of low rent video game quality. Del Toro holds up his end of the bargain but he is left completely and totally alone on the acting front lines. Emily Blunt, whom I have a giant crush on, just doesn't get much of a chance to do anything as her part is basically pointless. And then there's Hopkins. Oh, Anthony Hopkins, how I miss you. Hopkins gives one of the most mailed-in performances I have ever seen. He is completely uninspired and uninterested in his role, the movie, and maybe even breathing. I equate the last 10 years of Hopkins' career to that of Oakland Raiders owner Al Davis in that both were GREAT in their day but now it's unclear if either is still alive. It's like Hopkins died years ago but his family, in need of some quick cash, has reanimated his body and run a scam on the whole of Hollywood, continuing to sign his name to awful movies. Del Toro and the occasional glimpse of inspired FX keep "Wolfman" from being a total loss but it's not far off.Check out my site: www.thesoapboxoffice.blogspot.com</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A solid remake, though not as good as the original</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Wolfman (1941)was an undeniable classic, and without a doubt, the greatest were-wolf movie ever made. There was absolutely no way that the remake could match up to the original. But, the good thing about the remake was that it changed quite a number of things from the original. However, some of the changes were not so much a good idea. For example, Larry Talbot (or Lawrence) in the remake is not as like-able as the original, Benicio Del Toro does a great job as a man who is going to become a were-wolf, but he doesn't play the role as a character you feel sorry for him that much when he transforms, when in the original, you do. Maybe its just the writing, but none the less, Del Toro does a great job, but not as good as Lon Chaney Jr. Another thing it changes from the original, is that John Talbot is a were-wolf. And no, not the "I am so sad because I am a were-wolf", the "I have gone insane with the power of a were-wolf". Seriously, the guy is unpredictable, first he's trying to help his son, then he leaves him to become a were-wolf, then he just lets him get sent to an asylum, then he tells him to commit suicide, then he beats him, then the two of them have a were-wolf brawl, THE GUY IS UNPREDICTABLE! Which leads me to another thing I like about the changes made. This is probably the first movie to have a freakin' WERE- WOLF BRAWL! Seriously, a were-wolf brawl. The were-wolf brawl is the scene in the remake that, in a way, replaces the fight between The Were-wolf and Sir John at the end of the original. It is so over-the-top! One of the highlights of the movie, an absolute must-see moment if you are a were-wolf fan.Other changes include the useless characters. However, this just adds to the new movie's "charm". The villagers are all disposable. All horror movies need "the disposables", or useless characters that are there to either cause trouble or get killed. The original didn't have much death scenes or death of villagers, maybe two or three. But the remake wastes no time with the deaths of the disposables. It makes them as quick and as awesome as possibleThe death scenes in the movie are sadistic, or in other words, perfect for a were-wolf movie. There's not too much gore, and the movie knows how to balance a good death scene with enough gore.The story is also great, though a tad different than the original. The movie changes the story, and still manages to keep the original story intact. It adds new characters and keeps the old ones. There is a specific balance the movie holds with the boundaries of the original and remake.Overall, in the end, The Wolfman is a solid remake (something that is very rare in these days). It changes things, benefits from most of them but suffers from few of them. Among the horror movie wannabes of the 21st century, The Wolfman, in my opinion, is the only one that is truly worthy to be called a horror movie.If you are a horror movie fan looking for a breath of fresh air after all the horror movie wannabes of the 21st century-like me, then give this movie a watch. If you are a gore hound looking for a very bloody movie, you won't find it here. If you want to see what has to be one of the greatest were-wolf movies of all time, right up there with "The Howling", "Bad Moon", "American Were-Wolf in London" and "The Wolfman (1941)", then this movie is an absolute must-watch.</fullReview>

		<score>0.5</score>
		<shortDescription>Hollywood injects their formula into The Wolfman story</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>You can always count on Hollywood to keep the remake machine turning and churning, always making sure it rehashes something that's been sitting on the burner for too long. This time it was 1941's The Wolf Man starring Lon Chaney Jr. Almost 70 years after the original 2010's The Wolfman retells the story of Lawrence Talbot, an American who is bitten by a dangerous wolf creature while visiting his father in England. The creature's bite curses him and he now turns into a wolf man monster every full moon. The movie is about what you would expect from another Hollywood remake as long as you're not expecting much. This film is your typical remake that isn't exactly that great, but not terrible either.I'll start with the positives of this film and those and simply the visuals. The film starts out bleak and gloomy and makes sure to stay that way the entire rest of the film. Most of the architecture of the film is pretty typical 19th century English sculpture and architecture, but we do see it all in a dark and shadowy atmosphere which makes it interesting. Then of course there are the numerous forest scenes which go a little overboard on the fog, but can you blame them? More fog equals more fun. It is also, for the most part, a well shot film. There is a lot to be said for any film that comes out in the ridiculous shaky-cam era that presents its action sequences clearly. Most action or horror films it is impossible to tell what's going on but thankfully The Wolfman doesn't have this problem. I have to give kudos for that.The visuals of this film coupled with the amount of gore furthermore makes it all the better. The gore is another thing I can applaud this film for. It isn't afraid to be graphic. It does try to shock the viewer with some of the graphic scenes and maybe I'm just desensitized, but those moments only made me laugh rather than gasp. For whatever reason watching the wolf man swipe the head off of a man and watching blood spurt out of the bloody neck stub just makes me instinctively laugh hysterically. And for me there's no problem with this, I just wish there had been more.This leads me to all of the negatives of this here film. First off we have a script that leaves something to be desired. The dialouge has a range from cheesy silly to lame redundant. There are a few moments where you will nod your head in approval, but this films dialouge is very dry, very one dimensional, and very dull. The characters are mostly the same way. This film has no shortage of fine actors. Benicio Del Toro plays Talbot, the lead character. He starts out dreary and uninteresting but eventually grows to fairly enticing and all of the werewolf drama around him makes him exciting enough. Overall it's a surprisingly weak effort from Mr. Del Toro. Emily Blunt plays the lead actress in an utterly useless role that serves as nothing more than a love interest for Talbot, a storyline which is utterly pointless to the films progression. I like Emily Blunt and have nothing against her but she is probably the weakest point of the film. Not because of her acting but simply because of her poorly written character. Anthony Hopkins is probably the best actor in the film and his character is pretty interesting. He delivers some of the only memorable lines of the film and he's probably the most fun character to watch. Hugo Weaving also does a good job here but his character is introduced way too late in the film for the role to reach its full potential.Lastly there is just a major issue with the whole development of the story and how this film is constructed overall. My biggest issue is the pacing of the film which jumps around all over the place. I might have been able to stand this flaw a little more if it hadn't played out exactly how I didn't want it to. Basically, the film spends way too much time on certain aspects and rushes through others. We're watching The Wolfman for the exciting monster story about how a man is bitten by a werewolf and then becomes one himself, subsequently going on a killing spree. What we're not watching The Wolfman for is a weak romantic story between two uninteresting and one dimensional characters. The film rushes through some of the most quintessential scenes of the whole film, such as when Talbot is actually bitten by the werewolf. You know, the scene that sets the entire plot into motion. The scene is cool while it lasts, but it only lasts for a few minutes in order to make room with some painfully cheesy scenes between Del Toro and Blunt and their useless romantic subplot.The Wolfman could have been really cool, but instead it will only be remembered as another lackluster Hollywood remake, or better yet, not remembered at all. Visually it is a very neat film that can be a lot of fun to watch at times. But it fails everywhere else. I didn't hate this film, but I didn't like it either. It's not hard to enjoy something about it, but if you spend about five minutes actually analyzing it you'll realize how pointlessly flawed it really is.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>OK Werewolf Movie</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>What can I say about Werewolves? They're just plain awesome, and they're probably my number one favorite monster. An American Werewolf in London was an awesome Werewolf movie, besides the classic Wolfman movie, probably the best out there. Now don't get me wrong I didn't sit through The Wolfman (2010) thinking about either of those movies. I never compared once. I just wanted to be entertained. And some of the time, I was. I'm going to go over what I didn't like about the film first. Don't get me wrong, I think Benicio Del Torro is a fantastic actor. The transformation scenes were fun to watch, as they are always kind of the key scenes in Werewolf movies. Anthony Hopkins was great in this film, he was definitely the right choice for his character. He's so good at being dark and mysterious, he always leave you wondering what's going to happen next. I liked that the movie was a time period film. Having the film set in the 1800's definitely helped the dark mood of the film. The mood of the film did have that "classic" film feel, but with more gore. The gore was awesome, there enough of it without it being too gory. And I liked the ending, not giving anything away, but it leaves you wondering. ;) All in all, I didn't hate the film, but I didn't exactly like it either. I guess it's really up to the person who watches it. I give it a 6/10, because like I said, didn't hate, but didn't love it either.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A mixed bag of a film with enjoyment moments of violence</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Victorian England seems to be a gold mine for the film industry with many recent films set in that period: Sweeney Todd, Sherlock Holmes, The Prestige and Burke and Hare being examples. Universal have also decided to look at this period with a remake of it 40s horror film The Wolfman and hope to repeat the success of The Mummy.In 1891 Ben Talbot (Simon Merrells) is murdered by a mysterious creature in a English wood. His actor brother Lawrence (Benicio Del Toro) returns to his family home after receiving a letter from his brother's fiancée Gwen (Emily Blunt). Whilst looking for Ben's killer Lawrence is also attacked by the same creature and ends up suffering from the werewolf curse. Lawrence has to combat the beast within him, his father (Anthony Hopkins) with his own secrets and the Scottish Yard inspector Abberline (Hugo Weaving) who is hunting him.The Wolfman went through development hell. Mark Romanek was going to direct it but left, with Joe Johnston stepping in at the last minute. It was film that had to go through 40 days of pick-up shots, which is about half a film shoot and its release date was pushed back to February 2010, a dead zone for major film releases. Johnston is a perfectly servable director, he is a man who gets the job done and I did enjoy his film Jumanji when I was a child. But with the Wolfman there seems to be a little bit of a disconnect between him and the actors, Johnston seem to want to make a dark Gothic film whilst the actors treaded it as a cheese-fest. The art-direction and dark cinematography were expertly done to give a film a Gothic look and I liked the way the werewolf moved and strike. The film was at its best when it there was a large amount of violence with limps and heads being ripped off. Danny Elfman's score works best when the werewolf is attacking people. Johnston also shows that innocent people do get attack. Some elements of the film reminds me of Francis Ford Coppola's Dracula, a Gothic historical set horror film that even has scenes in a mental asylum. It was very similar in tone and style.The problem for me is with an undeveloped script and some weak casting choices. Many of the actors were obviously have fun whilst making the film like in the pub. Anthony Hopkins was in the film to chew up the scenery and give a strange cross between an English and Irish accent. Hugo Weaving gives his usual slightly sinister performance but he is best performer in the film: he is simply a police officer trying to protect the public. Emily Blunt tries to treat the film more seriously and tries to show that there is some depth of grief and love in her character. She is a sensitive soul who has to do the important, but whilst Blunt does an okay job I personally think she is a little overrated as an actress. Del Toro is the weakest link in the cast. He has proved himself before in many films, but he was miscast in The Wolfman. He could not do an English accent so the writers made out he was raised in America and he gives a lukewarm performance and it seems that he was in The Wolfman for a nice paycheck.Praise has to go to Rick Baker: he is one of the best make-up artists in the business. He makes an effective looking werewolf with fangs, claws and hair. Some people have been critical that he gave the werewolf a flat face but I actually like the classic look to the wolfman, making him a hybrid between a man and a wolf.An uneven film that does have some fun moments.5.5 out of 10.</fullReview>

		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>It's not cool but it is good</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Joe Johnstone's remake of the 1941 horror classic was a box-office bomb; Made on a budget of $150 million, the film managed to scrape back a peasy-assed $140 million-ish at the cinemas (that's worldwide, mind).The film seemed cursed (pun, as always, intended) before it's release as stories of directorial changes and production delays held the film up. Upon release the film met indifferent reviews and quickly disappeared from public conscience; but for some reason I felt an urge to give it a go.Maybe it was because I thought it would be good to see Benicio Del Toro headlining a 'mainstream' movie or maybe it was the fact the brilliant original is still the benchmark of werewolf movies but still old and quaintly archaic enough to warrant a 're-imagining'.Upon watching it's very easy to understand why the film failed to find an audience. The style of the movie is reminiscent of the Gothic spookiness of recent family friendly horror-lite such as Van Helsing or the Twilight series but the film contains enough blood, viscera and gore to warrant it's 15 certificate (R in the US). Though this was an attempt to give the film some much needed credence in the horror genre (the original is, after all, a pioneer of terror) compare it to the current swathe of 'torture-porn', the SAW movies and their copycat offspring and The Wolfman seems tame, like almost, well, a kid's movie.Away from the current trends of modern horror, The Wolfman stands out as a very entertaining, old-school, blood-and-guts romp. That's right, you heard me, 'ROMP'. You don't see enough films these days being referred to as 'romps'... But, I digress, whereas a lot of fantasy films these days have decided to maintain an air of po-faced seriousness, The Wolfman is a film that is aware of it's own sense of silliness and this undoubtedly is, in my opinion, a good thing.With each wolf-attack sequence seemingly trying to tick off an inventory of 'cool and creative ways of killing people' the action sequences, despite the odd bit of 'see-the-lines' CG are well-shot and superbly edited together.The style and tone of the film is impressive, with some tasty Gothic set design and a decent amount of creepy forests at night. The film's makers made a good decision to keep the film as a period piece as opposed to setting it in the modern day. It resembles an early Tim Burton film with creative use of shadows and colour (it also has a Danny Elfman score which adds to the Burton-esqueness) The acting is largely spot-on too, Del Toro maintains an air of tortured menace throughout, Emily Blunt does a good job despite her character being dragged out to show a bit of side-boob and offer a sympathetic ear to our titular monster and very little else. However, Anthony Hopkins and Hugo Weaving seem to be more than aware of the type of film they're making and are both clearly hamming it all the way to the bank, which benefits the film immensely.The film successfully updates the original without tarnishing the memory of the Universal horror classic, it largely maintains the same story as the original and keeps the same characters and is a worthy addition to the Werewolf sub-genre.The Wolfman is a rare treat, a piece of brainless escapism that keeps the audience gripped from start to finish, anyone with half a brain will spot the plot twists a mile off but that doesn't matter. It won't tax the brain or change the way you think about life and it won't have you thinking about it for any time afterwards but it succeeds in being an entertaining, dark and thrilling horror romp.That's right..... Romp.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>sddavis63 (revsdd@gmail.com)</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Standard Werewolf Fare; No Improvement On The Original</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>As werewolf movies go, this was pretty standard and didn't add very much to the genre. In fairness, it shouldn't be expected to "add" very much, I suppose. It is, after all, a remake of a classic - the 1941 version of the movie starring Lon Chaney. I always have mixed feelings when classic movies are remade, and usually find the remakes disappointing. In terms of writing a review, that means this movie has to be specifically looked at in comparison to the earlier version while at the same time considered in light of more modern versions of the werewolf legend.The story is very similar to the '41 movie. Set in 1891, Lawrence Talbot returns to England from America and gets caught up in a hideous nightmare after being attacked by the same creature that has already killed his brother. Even the gypsies are present. What made the classic Universal horror films from the 30's and early 40's classics was the use of the proper atmosphere. There was an attempt to hold on to that tradition with this. The scenes and sets seem appropriate and create an uneasiness for the viewer. On the other hand, this being 2010 the movie is much bloodier and more violent than the '41 version - a reflection of the modern tendency in such movies to replace "fright" with "gore." There is, indeed, gore galore in this version. Other differences revolve around some of the characters. Talbot's father (played by Anthony Hopkins) has a more prominent place and is a very different character than that played by Claude Rains in 1941 (this Talbot is a very mysterious and suspicious character indeed) and the character of the gypsy woman Maleva seemed more believable in 1941 (Maria Ouspenskaya handled the part better than did Geraldine Chaplin.) Other differences were more technical in nature. Special effects today are more advanced, so the primitive "transformation" scenes of '41 are replaced by far more believable transformation scenes, although the transformations are pretty routine by today's standards and don't really provide any shock.What worked well? The transformation in front of the doctors at the asylum. Lawrence had been committed there and was going to be brought out of his delusion of being a monster by being put in a restraining chair in front of the doctors during a full moon to prove to him that he wasn't a werewolf. That didn't work out as planned! The chase through the rooftops of London was pretty good, and the battle near the end between the two werewolves (Talbot's and the one that killed his brother) was brief but well executed. What didn't work well was the very predictable "twist" in the story. Unlike the '41 version we're going to learn the identity of the original werewolf. Unfortunately, you figure out who it is pretty early on, so mystery is lacking. As far as performances go, in all honesty Benecio Del Toro is no Lon Chaney, Jr. Chaney did a much better job of capturing the fear, bewilderment and anguish of Talbot coming to terms with what he's becoming than Del Toro did. Hopkins was fine as Talbot, Sr., but was primarily there to be a big name - another actor could have pulled the part off easily enough. One also hopes that the producers don't follow up on the opening for a sequel left at the end, as the inspector from Scotland Yard is bitten but not killed. This really wasn't good enough to warrant a sequel, but of course if Universal figures it can make money out of one, one will appear in due course.As werewolf movies go, this was OK. There are both better and worse ones out there. If you like werewolf movies, you'll probably like this. If you don't, it won't be of much interest to you.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A remake of a classic horror film done right!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>In this remake of the classic Universal film of the same name, BENICIO DEL TORO stars as Lawrence Talbot, who arrives to the home of his youth to stay with his father (Played wonderfully by ANTHONY HOPKINS) following his brothers horrible death, by what appears to be done by an insane lunatic or a wild beast. One night he is bitten by what appears to be a very large wolf or something that resembles one &amp; soon finds himself changing into a hideous werewolf creature by the light of the full moon who promptly disposes of anyone or anything, he comes across while travelling through the town, or in the woods, having seemingly no memory of what he has done when he changes back to human form &amp; more frighteningly, no memories of the people he knows when he's in wolf form, causing many of his friends &amp; enemies, to be ripped to shreds. Soon the woman he loves learns from an elderly gypsy woman that the werewolf curse is irreversible &amp; the only way to end the terror is death &amp; what's worse, besides Lawrence learning the truth, the heroine discovers a horrifying secret about just who the other werewolf that bit him is...THE WOLFMAN is pretty decent, though as unlike a lot of other "remakes" (see the horrible MUMMY (1999)) this one doesn't try to hide the fact that it is a horror movie &amp; isn't scared to provide horror fans with some good juicy gory bits with plenty of off the wall kills. The werewolf suits were awesome &amp; definitely resembled the original wolf man look, which I was really happy about. Another good thing this film did right was in the period setting, with a keen attention to detail resulting is some beautiful imagery &amp; sets. The cinematography was very well done here with plenty of mood &amp; atmosphere that give even some of the daylight scenes a sense of dread. The suspense &amp; kills are very well handled here &amp; the film boasts some spectacularly good acting &amp; directing as well. That aside, the film does have some flaws, most notably the films overlong running time, which could have (&amp; should have) been cut shorter to make a stronger tighter thrill ride that I felt would have worked better simply because the film at times does tend to drag on in places &amp; the films supposed "twist" should come to no surprise to anyone who has seen their fare share of horror flicks. In short, I think what else impressed me about the movie, was the fact that for once you actually got the feeling both while watching the movie &amp; after viewing it, that you've seen a good horror movie, not a great horror movie mind you, but a pretty darn good one. Unlike a lot of remakes done for the greed factor that really shows in all areas of production, you can see with this film that the studio &amp; film- makers actually cared for the material &amp; wanted to put out the best film they could for genre fans &amp; the film definitely earn my respect on that level. Thankfully Universal learned their lesson after the horrible MUMMY 1999 &amp; it's two just as bad &amp; as boring sequels, &amp; made up for those horrible mistakes with this one. Highly recommended to fans of the original Universal classic, STEPHEN SOMMERS &amp; BRENDAN FRASER can keep their lousy Mummy "remake" THE WOLFMAN is a real &amp; honest remake of the classic that I want to see!***1/2 stars</fullReview>

This has always been a wonderful story among the Gothic romances (the three standouts being Dracula, Frankenstein, and Wolfman) but definitely the most difficult one to interpret. The Gothic romantic horrors are difficult as they are overblown and melodramatic. Writing these can come off as laughable soap opera all too easily. They are love stories first and foremost with antiheroes in the forefront. Our romantic lead turns into a wolf and eats people (hardly a winning quality on Lavalife) yet we are to root for him and the tragedy that has befallen him. In this film we do. The script is weak to be sure but it is strong enough and held up by a stellar cast (although I think that Anthony Hopkins only plays Hannibal Lechter anymore) that we do want the Wolfman to overcome and win. Visually, the movie is spectacular. It is beautiful to watch. So what's wrong? It's boring. This movie is flawed. In spite of all that I have said it fails to grip you. There is no tension. It is overlong and tedious. At it's best, it is visually stimulating but at it's worst, it is celluloid Nyquil. Too bad. Could have been great.</fullReview>

The best parts of the film, fortunately, were the two main actors. Anthony Hopkins brings a mysterious elegance to every role and here he creates a character kind of like a spin-off from his character in Francis Ford Coppola's Dracula(a much better film by the way). But, the casting of Benicio del Toro as the tormented actor-turned hairy beast Lawrence Talbot was a very good move. Del Toro's ability to create a somewhat sympathetic and interesting character certainly saved a lot of face for this film, which by the final act consists of constant CGI effects getting in the way of the story trying to create real uneasiness.There were problems, but I must say that the good outweighs the bad in this case. Emily Blunt is fine in her supporting role, though underused, and Hugo Weaving is terrific as the relentless Inspector Aberline from Scotland Yard. I can't say I fully enjoyed this movie, but if you're in the mood for a very gory action feast with a few startling moments, The Wolfman delivers on a basic level.</fullReview>
