		<shortDescription>Father-absent series: The Wolfman.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>With two striking genius as the leads(Both won Oscar), Visual effects geek Joe Johnston as the director, The Wolfman entered our sights with its 1930 roots of gentlemen-ship, new millennium gore and very primitive brutality. However, the highlight was still the twisted father-son relationship.This is a review written under "Father-absence" theme, please also find my other reviews in this series to get an insight into Asians' perceptions on popular movies.All the classic monster movies got this birthmark, that the idea is conceived, inspired by absence of fathers/bad fathers in real life. Frankenstein was a story of "sins of the father's" which is told in a forwarded/futuristic narration. Dracula movies usually features a Dracula who likes to pretend being a loving father, yet featuring religious fanatics of Christianity as worst villains. "Interview with the Vampire" features everlasting homosexual "love" and a failed attempt to be a father figure. However, so far there's no movies like The Wolfman that directly writes the father-son relationship into such an escalating catastrophe. Guess the werewolf movies are all mirrors to our bloodthirsty natures/craziness/sins in a way.Into the 20th century, Darwinism was just at its dawn to overtake half the scientific world as well as spiritual. Early mechanics and technique in treating psychological disorders were also introduced, while the Church and the protestants strive to maintain their voices in this shape-shifting world. Caught up in the middle were the Gypsies who neither gave in to spiritual resorts provided by churches nor succumb to scientific mindsets that translate the whole world into mechanisms. Out of his passion to save his little brother, celebrated actor Lawrence Talbot traveled back to the gritty homeland where his estranged father awaited for him. He tracked down his brother's murderer into the Gypsy camp, where a mysterious creature with big fangs wrecked havocs around ripping a lot people apart. Bitten by this monster, Lawrence found himself gradually transforming into a werewolf as the moon reaches full. Deliberately releasing him in the night of final transformation, his suspicious father turned him in to the bureaus. Finally revealing his status as the werewolf that killed his own wife, Laurence's mother, Old Talbot invited his son for a final duel at home...... In the meantime, the beautiful Gwen also set out for the cure/redemption for her beloved Laurence.Other than an extraordinary tolerance to gore, you will need the ability to read between the lines to understand what The Wolfman tries to tell you. It is quite an interesting illustration of a splitting world that will never make the same sense again. No matter which lot they represent, the mob are always ignorant, unorganized and downright vulnerable. They can neither get a grasp of what's going on nor get the final answer to the origins of this curse/craziness/sins/anomaly, despite their unique claims to do so. Yet at the core of the movie, the old father repeatedly quotes the "Prodigal son parable" from New Testament. He had every due rights to call his son a "prodigal son" as the traditionally-upheld father(ancestor), yet ruthlessly passing on his sins/disease/curse/craziness to his poor family. Walking on water, the young widow set up a temporary alliance with the Gypsies, the only group who provided help and some goodwill of "saving grace". It's interesting that the story verified Gypsies and Gwen's love for Lawrence because despite they don't present any ideology, their love and compassion were both humble. Unlike Gwen and the Gypsy woman, the other groups were all holding up their own egos, just like any organized religion/group/party like to uphold "flags of their fathers". In this sense, the movie is not only illustrating the father-absence status of the 20th century, the movie's even rationalizing it with the notion of women's ability to pass on the only ultimate goodness, favorably the pagan goodness in an esoteric pattern. I haven't seen the original 1941 version, but I can surely tell it must have been quite an achievement among other post-modern stories that struggle to seek purity, clarity and maybe, redemption in a crazy world.This phenomenon, however, has been obstinate in the humanity. If secrecy, ambiguity in conducting and other esoteric characteristics could all be verified as "humility", then I guess one would invite evil beliefs into his/her life. In the city I live, there are thousands of fortune-tellers who make a good living on people's broken souls. As a Christian, I admit it might be unfair for one to admit and cleanse his ancestors' sins as well as the world's sins that descended upon him. Indeed, just like Lawrence we are heirs to sins/curses rather than heirs to happiness/innocence in this crazy world. However, if admitting one's sins and committing oneself to Jesus is truly the only way one can escape from being harvested by hell's fire, yelling "unfair" won't do you any good. While there's no clean getaway by simply conducting Gypsy relics, there certainly is a way to allow your soul to be saved. Besides, I haven't seen any werewolves, nor did I think people need to be monsters to be capable of monstrous deeds.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Dark, dark, dark...did I mention it's dark?</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I did not have high hopes for this film. It's a remake (which are often not very good), and it stars Benicio del Toro and Anthony Hopkins, two actors who couldn't bear any less resemblance to each other, as father and son. Even more troubling was it's troubled production history, which included a new director and tons of re-shoots and re-edits. The latter is mostly a sign of producer interference, which is never a good sign. But after all that, the resulting movie is actually pretty enjoyable.Before I go any further, I have to admit that I have not seen the original "Wolf Man" with Lon Chaney, so comparisons to the original will not appear here.London theater actor John Talbot (Benicio Del Toro) is visited by Gwen Conliffe (Emily Blunt), the wife of John's brother, Ben. Ben has disappeared without a trace, and this comes after three brutal murders. Naturally, she's worried, so she has come to John for help. But John is unwilling because of a fractured relationship with his father. Nevertheless, he eventually returns home, and while there, whispers of a werewolf begin to emerge. No one takes it seriously, but John does after he's bitten.The acting is solid. Benicio Del Toro is effective as John, the protagonist. We understand his emotions, and Oscar Winner Del Toro knows what he's doing. Despite obvious deficiencies in their casting, Del Toro has terrific chemistry with each of his co-stars, especially Hopkins. Hopkins is perfect in this role, being alternately aloof and creepy. The script is well-written, but Hopkins manages to be better than it. Emily Blunt fares the best as Gwen, whose earnestness manages to tug the heartstrings. Hugo Weaving, however, is miscast, but he's not given much screen time.Joe Johnston made a number of unmemorable action pictures. They were good, but disposable. Here, Johnston's reigns are loosened (the R-rating, which he takes full advantage of, helps a lot), and he fully throws himself into the material. The first thing one recognizes is the atmosphere, which is so thick that it threatens to drown out the actors. The second is the set design, which is impeccable. Even if the film didn't have a good story or actors, the film is visually pleasing enough to be worth it. All of this is due to Johnston. Finally, there's the actual Wolfman design by master make-up artist Rick Baker. It's actually rather beautiful, in a sick and twisted way. The transformations, however, are of a different story. The CGI is unconvincing, and is reminiscent of the late summer action movies that are only eye candy (and not very good ones at that).The film gets off to a rather slow start, but it reminded me of "Brotherhood of the Wolf," which helped (although the similarities with the French film end after the first half hour). Still, this part is interesting because of its darkness. But once it hits its stride, it unleashes a number of plot twists and surprising revelations. And some creepy action sequences.In short, "The Wolf Man" is as visually pleasing and interesting as any monster movie I have seen in recent release.</fullReview>

If you're not a particular fan of the genre, you probably won't like it; if you are you should know better than to not appreciate it for its obvious high production values. If you're going to get overinvolved with comparisons with this cast to the looks of the original I offer this observation - if you don't think Del Toro looks enough like Chaney Jr to satisfy you, compare him with Chaney Sr. His cheekbones and jawline are greatly reminiscent of Lon Sr's most memorable characterization!! I saw it theatrically twice and have just watched the unrated version on blu ray. The expanded version is more interesting, but makes me wonder why an actor of Max Von Sydow's status got left out of the credits of that edition.</fullReview>

		<username>dee.reid</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>"The Wolfman" - A fabulous-looking disappointment</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Bias alert: Let me just say that I in no way expected Joe Johnston's "The Wolfman" to be a movie that I would end up liking. In an era where everything seems to be an adaptation/remake/reboot/sequel, "The Wolfman" just seemed to be the latest Hollywood quick-cash-grab, where laziness has overtaken any semblance of artistic credibility and creativity.On top of that, "The Wolf Man" (1941) is probably the greatest werewolf movie ever made; the lead character in that film, Larry Talbot, was made famous by the great Lon Chaney, Jr. My own personal favorite werewolf adventure is the landmark horror-comedy film "An American Werewolf in London" (1981), which starred David Naughton as the tragic American college student-turned-werewolf in London David Kessler, and in my opinion set a standard for special effects in horror films that has yet to be topped.In 2010, we got "The Wolfman." Of the classic Universal Pictures movie monsters, the Wolf Man was always my favorite. Werewolves are the grungiest and most tragic of those monsters because they became monsters unwillingly, and their stories always ended tragically. On top of that, these particular monsters have always been featured using some of the best special effects of their times, which also added to my love for these horrifically tragic creatures.In "The Wolfman," a bloodthirsty beast is stalking the woods and town of Blackmoor, England, in 1898. Superstitious locals believe that the gruesome murders taking place in the area are the work of a werewolf. Gloomy American stage actor Lawrence Talbot (the gloomy Benicio Del Toro) is brought back to Talbot Estate after learning of the death of his brother, where his estranged father Sir John Talbot (Anthony Hopkins) still resides. Haunted by many childhood dramas - witnessing the death of his mother at a young age, namely - he resolves to find out what happened to his brother but in his search for answers, he meets his late brother's fiancée Gwen (Emily Blunt) and runs afoul of the mysterious Scotland Yard Inspector Abberline (Hugo Weaving, still in "Agent Smith" mode), who has been assigned to the recent spree of mutilations. To make long stories short, Lawrence finds himself bitten by the beast and learns that he is thus accursed by the mark of the werewolf, and that there is indeed "a fate worse than death.""The Wolfman" has to be one of the greatest-looking horror movies to come out in a long while. The film's cinematography is very evocative of the time and mood. Everything is washed down in a careful and hazy shade of rain, fog, clouds, moonlight, and the color blue. I admired the cinematography, production (really Gothic, huh?), and atmosphere so much that I just couldn't look away at all, no matter how much I felt that this whole thing was just some sort of cash-grabbing rehash. This is clearly one of the best and most atmospheric 19th-century England's I've ever seen in the movies. It really made me think of "The Wolf Man" from 69 years ago.The movie really has top-of-the-line actors working their way through desperately rehashed material. We know where things are going in the end, even if 2010 technology has not allowed storytelling techniques to advance any further than they should. And speaking of special effects, imagine my horror when I learned that "An American Werewolf in London" special effects legend Rick Baker was attached to this film's production as its make-up effects guy! The previews had convinced me that this new Wolf Man would be all CGI and nothing else, so imagine my surprise when this new Wolf Man is actually the work of some careful, good-old fashioned prosthetics and make-up work, in addition to good-old fashioned CGI.This new Wolf Man exists as both CGI and a man in heavy make-up. What's disappointing about the CGI is that he is rarely convincing in the scenes where he is running around and ripping people limb from limb. To paraphrase Roger Ebert's take on him, he moves around too much like a character in a live-action cartoon; "Spider-Man 2" (2004), my personal favorite superhero film and my #2 film of all time, set a standard for CGI in the movies by allowing Spider-Man to possess weight and dimension as he swung from building to building, thus making him seem "real" within the context of the film. The Wolf Man in "The Wolfman," like I stated earlier, moves to much like he exists in a comic book world, rather than the real world, because he doesn't possess the qualities that would make him seem like a real character.I think back to the transformation sequences from "The Wolf Man" and "An American Werewolf in London," where make-up effects, camera-work, clever editing, and animatronics were what allowed the werewolves in those movies to come to life and seem like real movie characters upon themselves, and they were frightening back then; the ability of the characters in those films to scare you hasn't diminished in the years it has taken them to become established movie monsters."The Wolfman" is good on its own terms. It looks fantastic, has great performances from the cast, but lacks any real depth or imagination. I guess that since I'm a sucker for werewolf movies, I had to give it a chance, despite my biases, and it does work, like I said, on its own terms. I just wish that Hollywood would give originality and creativity a stronger chance in the future.6/10</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>The Classic Movie Monster is Revamped, But Not Improved</shortDescription>

The film also has make-up wizard Rick Baker at its disposal, who has made countless jaw-dropping creatures and effects. 		<date>2010-02-28</date>
		<username>pyrocitor</username>
		<score>0.4</score>
		<shortDescription>Yet another embarrassingly overdone and ineffective butchering of a horror classic</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>If there is one thing to be said about director Joe Johnston's remake (or 'reimagining', as is the usual bland justification) of The Wolf Man, it is that it ably reinforces the benefits of trusting first impressions. First word of the project had led to snarls of discontent from fans of the original (surely not another horror classic destroyed!) to general unease (do we really need another horror remake?), yet star Benicio Del Toro's insistence on lavish respect for the original to the point of utilizing practical effects (an actual man in fur rather than a cop-out CGI creation) sated many complaints and many began to cultivate genuine excitement for that rare horror film willing to stay faithful to its origins. And, of course, such expectations immediately crumbled upon the release of a work even worse than initial pangs of hesitation could have suggested. For Johnston's The Wolf Man, rather than a classier addition to the contemporary horror pantheon instead proves one of its most tasteless entries: a sensationalized, senseless, shambling mess.While the 1941 Lon Chaney Jr. original proved elegantly effective in its skeletal simplicity, its retread appears bewildered as to how to remain allegiant to the source text while still delivering on the demands of contemporary horror. Rather than choosing one or the other, or allowing one to skillfully bleed into the other, the film's screenplay, with all the tact and sophistication of a hyperactive three-year-old with a hammer, only succeeds in crafting a film which disgracefully fails at both objectives. While the obvious (and most successful) approach would have been to devote further focus on the titular character itself, allowing for further drama, pathos and poignant inclusion of thematic temporal concerns befitting the genre, the film instead opts for the banal inclusion of yet another wearisome 'doppleganger' antagonistic conflict, and is dementedly content to simply shove said plot strand into the narrative, derailing any flimsy sense of pacing or cohesion which may once have existed.Similarly, the film does not even prove self-aware enough to embrace its own 'so-bad-it's-good' campiness factor, instead vying for 'serious credibility' with gruesomely realistic violence and gore yet overusing them with the reckless abandon of a splatterhouse comedy, evoking a glaring contrast between the excesses of over-sensationalized carnage and the hilariously plodding dialogue, nonexistent characterisations and Danny Elfman's embarrassingly clichéd and downright useless musical score. To give Johnston his due, he does craft a fittingly grim and Gothic atmosphere of fog and shadows, even if his frequent high speed time lapse shots of the moon changing wrench any subtlety or effectiveness from sequences aspiring at building tension. Otherwise, only a single nightmare sequence and the initial transformation scene generate any fumblings of fear or quality to retain interest, the rest being swamped by an atrocious mass of contemptible shoddiness and complacency.Even the superb cast prove universally awful, their feeble attempts at performances only serving to sink the film further into its quagmire of wretchedness. The normally stellar Benicio Del Toro delivers his tormented Lawrence Talbot all the range and nuance of a brick wall, mumbling his lines as if asleep and substituting blank confusion for any foreseeable emotion. While he may perk up somewhat when clad in wolf fur, his beady-eyed snarlings alone are not enough to salvage anything foreseeable as a proper performance. As Talbot's reclusive father, Anthony Hopkins, rather than contributing dignity and class, is instead a shamefully uncomfortable watch, his leering and cackling like a deranged goblin (even parodying his own Hannibal Lector voice at one point) instantly destroying any hopes of keeping his 'surprise villainy' a secret, and the sight of him transforming into a pudgy werewolf proving the most unintentionally hilarious moment of the film. Emily Blunt is simply a nonentity of a presence as Talbot's haunted love interest, delivering a performance intangible enough to seemingly fade off the screen, and even Hugo Weaving, attempting to have some fun chewing the scenery as a caustic, suspicious police inspector feels only irritating and unnecessary.A suspense film foolish enough to bank its tension on twists revealed oddly early, a horror film with effects and delivery too overdone to be horrific, and a psychological drama which instead comes across as a boorish farce - seldom has a film let down even its initial scanty potential as much as Johnston's astoundingly poor The Wolf Man. Oh, and to add insult to injury: even the practical werewolf effects, the main selling point of the film, are still dwarfed by, you guessed it, unconvincing CGI. 'Cursed' could hardly be a more fitting description.-4/10</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>amazingly enough, you want more of the human story and less of the monster in this surprisingly worthy remake of a classic horror film</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I was a little apprehensive at first of going into "The Wolfman" for two reasons. Number one, it was a remake and most of the remakes I've seen in the past ten years have ranged from mediocre to awful. Number two, because it was clearly going to be a gory horror film, which it is. But surprisingly enough, I enjoyed and even got caught up in this most effective remake but it does sink my heart a little as the masterpiece quality that it shows at many points never fully pans out to the full potential. But don't get me wrong. This is a very entertaining movie. Scary? No. But enjoyable? Absolutely.The plot and most of the story is the same as the 1941 original, with some very nice twists thrown into it. Lawrence Talbert (Benicio Del Toro) receives word that his brother is dead and comes to his mourning father (Anthony Hopkins) and his dead brother's fiancée (Emily Blunt). Then, as you'd figure it, Lawrence is one day bitten by a savage wolf-like animal and begins to suffer from panic attacks and strange animal-like symptoms and before long...well, you know.In my opening paragraph, I described to you that I think this movie shows masterpiece quality but does not reach it. This is what I meant. What I meant by that was that the parts that do work in this movie are wonderful and absorbing and the parts that don't work are very clichéd and a little dull. What works? The important parts: the story and the characters. You want more of the human characters and less of the hairy flesh-eating beast in this creature feature. I found the characters very interesting and I was very intrigued by the relationships between them. Usually in movies of this grade, all the characters are for is to stand around and be eaten. Here, I got involved with the romance between Benicio Del Toro and Emily Blunt, two very underrated professionals in the acting business and I was also intrigued by the relationship between Del Toro and his father played by the great Anthony Hopkins. What was also very cool, for me anyway, was the casting choices, because the new cast mirrors that of the 1941 original. Director Joe Johnston cast actors who not only resemble those in the original, but who had similar acting styles. Benicio Del Toro reminds us both in appearance and performance of Lon Chaney, Jr and Anthony Hopkins has a similar screen-capturing presence like Claude Rains did. A new addition to the cast is a hunter/psychiatrist played by Hugo Weaving, who is also very good in the picture even though his character's personality is completely stereotypical and two-dimensional."The Wolfman" is also done well on a technical standpoint. The cinematography is absolutely beautiful and it captures the feeling of an alternative reality that a movie like this needs to embrace. The art direction is fabulous as well. I loved looking at the movie as well as experiencing the subliminal qualities it had to offer. The music score is also, I'm glad to say, most effective whereas most soundtracks in contemporary horror films fail to be memorable.Now what didn't ring my bell? Well, the creature. The wolf-man. And this is why: because when the humans were on screen, I felt a refreshing presence, a welcomed substance in a horror film. When the creature came on-screen and started tearing people limb from limb and spraying stage blood all over the walls, I felt a little letdown because I felt I'd seen this all before. Now if they just done two things: 1) tone down the violence in these scenes and 2) space them further apart and allow more screen time with the characters then I think this would have risen to the stellar status of being a modern masterpiece. And the other problem with the gory moments? Well, they are overdone and repeated too often to the point where they no longer become scary.In the end, although I had a few pre-viewing concerns, "The Wolfman" turned out to be a very enjoyable picture. I only wished that the director had chosen to limit the amount of gory attacks in the movie and focus more on the human characters, for they were a lot more interesting than seeing the familiar routine of people being attacked out of nowhere and torn to pieces, spraying blood all over the set. And for those who are concerned about the blood and guts, don't worry. It's not very scary, mostly because again the attack scenes are too repetitive and familiar, and therefore, I think anybody over the age of ten should be able to cope with this. I enjoyed this remake of a classic horror film.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Not awful, just disappointing</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I spent this Valentine's Day with my significant other, treating her to a costly Indian dinner under one condition: we would see The Wolfman. It feels like years ago I first saw the trailer for this remake of the classic 1941 horror film. Indeed, the movie's production has been quite a horror story itself, and was plagued with reshoots and re-edits. I waited patiently through the delays and release date changes, so when it finally hit theaters, I knew I couldn't miss it.The story is simple, and closely follows the original and its late 19th century setting. Lawrence Talbot (Benicio del Toro), an actor living in America, returns home to England when he learns of his brother's mysterious death. He stays with his father, Sir John Talbot (Anthony Hopkins), who lives alone in a creepy old mansion that is in desperate need of a good dusting. There's also Gwen (Emily Blunt), the young widow grieving over the death of her husband, Lawrence's brother. Hmm, I wonder what will happen there? Anyway, Lawrence finds himself at a gypsy camp one night while the mysterious beast is about, and makes the mistake of venturing alone into a fog (a big no-no). He is bitten by the beast, and you can probably guess what happens next.Sadly, the story is never gripping; in fact, nothing about The Wolfman is. The first half of the film takes itself too seriously, and is nearly void of any action whatsoever. The second half ends up somewhere between Tim Burton and Sam Raimi. There are some good silly moments, but they are too inconsistent to be labeled "silly fun".I try not to let a film's production history shape my opinions, but I could not help but notice a serious case of overproduction here. The editing seems too choppy and there's too much of Danny Elfman's score. And why must filmmakers throw in a CGI bear when they've managed to create a werewolf nearly free of any computer effects? The Wolfman was directed by Joe Johnson, the man behind Jumanji and Jurassic Park 3. After watching this film, I admit I'm nervous about what he might do with the upcoming Captain America movie.The Wolfman is not an awful film, just disappointing. Considering the acting talent featured here, a much better movie was definitely possible.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>Movie_Muse_Reviews</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Familiar old school monster thriller with some modern movie trappings</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The goals of today's reboots have been to update the originals with the special effects of the modern era and add complexity and depth to cinema's most memorable characters. This is only half true for Universal's long-delayed "The Wolfman" remake. The full-moon transformations of man to werewolf are enhanced with top-notch gruesome CGI, but the man behind the beast remains uncomplicated. This "Wolfman" is a classic monster horror film with a modern pulse. Clichéd suspense and generic storytelling are king, only they're accompanied by extreme gore and curdling special effect for a more 21st Century thrill.It takes a bit for "The Wolfman" to clearly establish itself as a typical monster movie. The story jumps right in with actor Lawrence Talbot (Del Toro) being summoned back home to Blackmoor, England by his brother's fiancée (Blunt) because his brother has mysteriously disappeared. It's quickly after that we learn he's dead and suddenly Lawrence and Gwen have an affinity for one another. Then there's a quick side note assuring us Anthony Hopkins will indeed be creepy as the family patriarch, Sir John Talbot.After the initial werewolf shreds some guts and swipes off a few heads, it finally bites Lawrence the story really begins. Minus the trite dialogue using monster-related similes about how dangerous he is and how he doesn't know it and that Gwen should go before she gets hurt all while constant shots of the moon remind us of what will inevitably happen next, it's entertaining. The two very good scenes in the film happen toward the middle when Lawrence is captured and taken to an asylum in London. First he has a series of nightmarish flashbacks to childhood trauma as the staff tries those reputable ice bath and shock treatments. The best, however, is when the head doctor who believes him crazy wants to make an example of him before a lecture hall of colleagues and straps him to a chair while the full moon is set to show he's been cured. Of course that's not what happens at all. Comical gore ensues.Amid the typical monster shenanigans that result somehow in a wolf-on-wolf showdown, there are a few wasted talents. Emily Blunt is too good for this film and Hugo Weaving (Mr. Smith of "The Matrix" and V in "V for Vendetta") provides the film's only example of acting with subtext. Del Toro and Hopkins are more or less wasted although odd characters of questionable morals always come better from Hopkins than from just anybody."The Wolfman" is a big-budget monster horror movie and it deserves to be viewed with those kinds of expectations despite the reputation of its stars and the inherent trend of most films trying to reboot a classic going for the more serious, theme-driven stories. "Wolfman" just picks a couple interesting thematic lines and delivers them one more time just to sound legitimate. The film is entertaining despite the basic and surprise-less script. In a sense its shallow delivery is an homage to old-school horror films. It would have been nice to have gotten a more complicated retelling of the tragic tale of Lawrence Talbot, but it works just fine as a genre film.~Steven CVisit my site at http://moviemusereviews.com</fullReview>
	</review>

		<fullReview>The Wolfman is an adequate updating of the Universal franchise, with good and bad aspects.On the good side, it looks great. 		<username>Ben_Horror</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>A disappointing and uninspired updating of the werewolf myth</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>When this movie was going into production, it made the news for all the wrong reasons with its change of directors, re-shoots, unhappy star, etc. While hints of this can be seen in the somewhat disjointed nature of its plot, it still does not make excuses for its evident shortcomings. A remake of a much older film, it tells the story of an American man called Laurence Talbot (Benicio Del Toro - looking like a rather aged Leonardo Dicaprio) who journeys to England after his brother is brutally savaged to death by a strange creature while out on the moors. It transpires that this animal was a werewolf. In the course of his investigations into his brother's death, Del Toro gets attacked by the same creature, but somehow survives, almost certainly guaranteeing that come the next full moon, he too will turn into a similar monster?The film has all the gloss you would expect from a movie of its caliber and budget. It looks lovely with moody photography, Hammer-esquire style set dressing, foreboding locations and an effective, ever-present swirling cloud of Gothic mist during a lot of the night time sequences. There are also some neat time-lapse visual touches, which add to a very polished looking production. A pleasing amount of gore is on display and we're treated to decapitations, dismemberments and eviscerations galore. While there's absolutely no doubt to the workmanship, the problem here lies in the script. The movie starts off good, but somehow loses its way, never quite living up to the initial promise. Benito Del Toro is charismatic in his role and seems to have been born to play it, but the entire production just has a pointless, 'so what?' feel to it. The movie falls flat on its face maybe a third of the way in. It just seems to just run out of juice (a legacy of re-shoots, perhaps?), which is a shame due to the evident talent on board. For example, the romance between Del Toro and Emily Blunt is completely unconvincing: at the beginning of the movie she was in love with Del Toro's brother, but later on she's in love with Del Toro? And willing to risk her life for him even though she barely knows him and he's running around killing people? It's plot holes like these that can't stand up to scrutiny and give credence to the troubled creative process this no doubt had. Still it's a good thing Hugo Weaving is on hand as a detective from Scotland Yard to keep us all interested. He invests his role with the appropriate amount of grit. So much so in fact that without him, your interest in this movie would have seriously waned. On top of all this, there's also the fact that the filmmakers have opted for a rather ill advised decision, which becomes the movie's fatal flaw?If you're going to remake an old movie, you need to update it accordingly and therein resides the problem here: The Wolfman is too faithful to the original. Rather than giving us a werewolf design to knock our socks off, we instead get the same style as the original. He's still essentially a bipedal creature, albeit one with hugely superior make-up. While it is rather admirable that the filmmakers chose to go practical rather than CGI, we still just end up with a guy wearing clothes while covered in hair. Where's the point in that? Where is all the updating and the re-visioning? The sad fact is the werewolf is painfully retro in style. Why couldn't they have just let their imaginations go and given us something frightening? Why didn't they give us a slavering unholy monstrosity? As director if you're not going to change anything about The Wolfman and bring something unique to it, then why even bother? It is also rather telling that even though it was released in 1981, An American Werewolf in London still contains the best werewolf transformation scene ever done. Even with its in-camera animatronic effects, it still beats the transformation scene in this movie hands down. Granted, whatever CGI that is used here to bridge the metamorphosis from human to monster is done quite effectively, the resulting snarling hairy man still lets it down. To add further credence to the American Werewolf's superiority, the new movie even seems to contain a kind of homage to it in the scene where the creature runs amok and we see a tram/carriage keeling over much in the same way a Double Decker bus does in a similar scene from Piccadilly Circus in the 1981 movie. Worse of all, as this is a movie made to satisfy the comic book movie generation, it even has the obligatory one-on-one fight sequence between two superhuman creatures at the end. Except rather than a superhero and his nemesis, here we get two hairy supernatural beasts slugging it out. We've seen this type of scenario time and time again and frankly it's getting boring. Can a movie end with something other than a bout of 'fisty-cuffs' for a change? It's already been done to death in movies like Spiderman, Ironman and Wolverine et al.The movie hints at a (surprise, surprise) sequel. While this might be an unwelcome prospect for most, let's hope they at least have a better script than the one they went ahead with here. Oh yeah, and a complete overhaul of the werewolf design wouldn't go astray either.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>A mess of editing, but still campy fun</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Its rare these days for a monster movie to go for scares. Most either take the route of becoming action movies, or descend into campiness. The reason for this is simple: old school monster movies have lost their effect due to a desensitized audience. Todays viewers are well aware of the stuff movies can do, so effects alone can't convince. The wolfman, a semi-remake of one of the original classic monster movies, certainly doesn't bring the scares, but it has enough campy fun to be worth watching.The Wolfman certainly seems to be taking itself seriously at first. After his brother is found mutilated, estranged son Lawrence Talbot returns home to find the best that did it. Of course he finds the beast, and it bit by it, and inherits the curse. The scenes leading up to this are treated relatively seriously, with Lawrence's father waxing sentimental and his brother's fiancée being appreciative enough that we know the two will engage in some kinky inter-species nasty later on (spoilers: doesn't happen, though I'm sure it would have been a great moment for film).Then it all gets gleefully tossed aside when the werewolf attacks a gypsy camp, full earning its 18A rating in the most ludicrous showering of gibs since, well, Daybreakers. Backs are ripped off, heads are swiped clean, and organs are strewn about in all sorts of pretty ways. Its all well in fun, but its never scary. Indeed, camp is the natural replacement of scares in horror movies. In general, if one cannot frighten, throw in as much gore as humanly possible to get laughter and maybe the odd squick. The Wolfman does this in spades, but if thats the mood you're in it works splendidly.The editing nearly kills it though. While the camera is always steady and clear, its still difficult to comprehend what is going on because things just seem to happen. Its like the scenes were longer, and then essential bits just got thrown out to make the film shorter. The climactic wolf battle is a particularly egregious example, which resulted in plenty of "wtf's" from me.The plot is somewhat serviceable, though the lack of a strong villain is glaring. Not that Man VS Himself wouldn't work in this situation, just that they attempt to include a weak villain that never really grabs attention. A lot of the fault is on the characters. Del Toro plays it straight, which may have been the wrong choice for this film. Blunt is plenty ladylike, but forgettable. Hopkins is horribly disappointing as Lawrence's father, spouting off wannabe-epic dialogue that mostly falls flat or seems random. The bad guy (I'll keep it a secret) has one scene where he ineffectly lists off his bad deeds with the calm of someone reading groceries, seeming more lazy than evil. If he had said he kicked puppies and spat on the Pope I wouldn't be surprised. The only good performance comes from Hugo Weaving as Detective Freddy Aberline, inexplicably based on the real-life investigator of the Ripper murders. He has one particular crowning moment of awesome in a bar, but otherwise is underutilized.The Wolfman is no classic, but there's worse matinees to see. Its a complete and utter mess by some standards, but has enough campy charm to be more than enjoyable to laugh along with. Werewolves may be down and out for scares, but at least they can be relied on to maul things in brutally hilarious ways.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Del Toro is a man who is pure at heart and says his prayers by night . . . . . . . . .</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>All the signs were there. No director being found in time, reshoots, a release date pushed further and further back. Everyone had almost made up their mind before this movie was released that it was cloaked in the scent of failure. Well, I hope those people fight through their prejudgment and give this movie a try because it is actually a highly laudable remake and a bloody good film.Joe Johnston directs from a screenplay by Andrew Kevin Walker and David Self (based on the 1941 screenplay by Curt Siodmak) and werewolf fans will be pleased to hear that Rick Baker (THE werewolf guy thanks to his amazing work in An American Werewolf In London) is also involved.Benicio Del Toro plays Lawrence Talbot, a travelling stage actor who returns home after he is informed of his brother's disappearance. Once there, he resumes the difficult relationship he has with his father (Anthony Hopkins) and gets to know his brother's fiancé (Emily Blunt) while also trying to unravel the mystery surrounding the event that brought him back to the family home. When the full moon rises, things get hairy and bloody once more and the locals are up in arms, full of nothing but suspicion for the nearby visiting gypsy folk and ready to blame the most convenient scapegoat for the horrors they cannot comprehend. Inspector Abberline (Hugo Weaving) seems to be the only figure of calm in an increasingly turbulent situation but will he remain so unflappable as the evidence piles up before his eyes? It's really hard to say whether or not this movie will appeal to those who have already grown up with such love for the original movie that it's based on (The Wolf Man, one of the Universal classic horrors) but I'd like to think that it will. Yes, it falls between two stools in many ways with it's blend of CGI and practical effects, it's pacing issues and it's focus on character more than the actual monster madness but that's what fans of classic horror movies have been used to in the past. And for those worried about the use of the computer effects, let me just say that while it IS noticeable in many places I have to say that this movie finally provides the blending of the new technology with the old in a way I have been hoping for ever since CGI started to cast it's large, large shadow.In the acting department, Del Toro gives what may well be his very best performance. Of course, that will be neglected and overlooked by most people as this is "simply" a horror movie but his portrayal of Talbot sums up the pain and tragedy of the character while also echoing the mighty Chaney Jr's performance from the original movie. It's simply superb. Anthony Hopkins is a lot of fun and livens up every scene he's in, Emily Blunt does very well and Hugo Weaving is a lot of fun in every scene he gets. In fact, everyone does very well . . . . be they travelling gypsies, arrogant psychiatrists or nervous locals.As for the rest of the movie, it seems to throw in many different flavours that somehow blend to create something wonderful. My rating may seem comparatively low compared to the praise here but that's subject to change upon future viewings. We get some lush sets and design work, some fantastic FX sequences, a wealth of sly little winks to fans of the lycanthrope subgenre and so much more than all of those bad omens mentioned in my opening paragraph would suggest. It may be far from perfect in many ways but, thank goodness, it certainly gets an awful lot right in a package that almost provides something for everyone. A new pup has nuzzled it's way into the pack.See this if you like: The Wolf Man, An American Werewolf In London, Werewolf Of London.</fullReview>

It becomes clear quite early , that Anthony Hopkins is not the model for fatherhood, and that there is no cure for Lycanthropy. I gave this film a 7, despite its' goofiness, and not having the ability to stay out of its' own way. See it with a GREAT AMOUNT of Suspension of Disbelief !or wait for the DVD/Blu-Ray.</fullReview>

		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Looks like this werewolf fell into the GAP...</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I never understood what the term pedestrian meant when applied to film. Did it mean a movie would, on occasion, cross a street? If so, did it have the right of way? Well now that I've seen "The Wolfman", I've finally got a firm grasp of what this means.There was nothing really lacking in the performances. Benicio Del Toro did a good job as the cursed Lawrence Talbot?it didn't hurt that he just happens to look like Lon Chaney. Anthony Hopkins ran the razor's edge between paternal concern and menace as John Talbot. Rounding out the rest of the cast, Emily Blunt, Hugo Weaving, Art Malik, and Geraldine Chaplin all did their part in attempting to suspend my disbelief.The script was noticeably rushed in spots, but this was a monster movie and not Shakespeare. This is why the hurried pace of the burgeoning romance between Lawrence and Gwen Conliffe (Blunt) wasn't much of a problem for me. The shift between the slower, more suspenseful tone of the first two acts and the frenzied tone of the third one didn't bother me either. The only problem I had was John Talbot. Given his role in the narrative, it would've made this film more complete to have seen more of his story and how he goes from being a reluctant monster to a full time bastard. Yet, even if they had done this, there was still something noticeably off about this presentation.This is a remake of a film which was ancient over twenty years before I was born, and I'm no spring chicken. There was absolutely no effort made to move the story forward at all. The setting of the Moors and London, circa 1891, just didn't work for me. Yeah, they were picturesque and spooky. But they probably would've fared better had they looked more naturalistic, more contemporary, and less like a soundstage. Due to this, they only served to move this story faaar from reality. And, "An American Werewolf in London" showed how much more engaging this type of story can be when it takes place in the world we know.Speaking of AAWIL, too much of "The Wolfman" seemed to borrow from Landis' 1981 masterpiece. The tavern and its patrons looked too much like transplants from the Slaughtered Lamb?all that was missing was a pentagram and a dartboard. Then there was the werewolf rampage in London. Yet another bus was made to run over someone in order to avoid striking a snarling lycanthrope. Yet another crowd of people parted like the red sea as a monster tore through their ranks. Yet another assemblage of policemen gathered as they tried, in vain, to contain the beast.All of this leads to what was at the heart of why this movie dragged along?the monster itself. After all, what is a monster movie with a lackluster creature at its core? Despite all the work put into keeping the creature's appearance in line with the 1941 version, it just doesn't work. Yes, the transformation scene was surprisingly well thought out, with blood dripping from Talbot's mouth and hands as fangs and claws pushed their way through the skin. Yet, when I first got a clear look at the wolfman, I didn't see a werewolf. To me, it looked more like a hirsute, young republican. Seriously, a lycanthrope that's shooting the cuffs ain't scary and hasn't been since the sixties. This is why, as soon as the F/X technology allowed for it, this model was cast aside.This conceptual misstep was only compounded by the involvement of Rick Baker. If Landis was the right hand of AAWIL, then Baker was the left. He knew how outdated the traditional design had become, and was instrumental in its re-envisioning. This is why I'm shocked he would opt to take a major step backwards from his previous effort. The dude should've put his foot down and said this design wasn't going to hold up to his other werewolf film. After all, he's Rick "Futher Muckin" Baker, the guy who the Oscar committee created the makeup and F/X category for. If his opinion doesn't hold any sway, then Hollywood truly is run by flat-scans.All in all, "The Wolfman" had serviceable acting and a story that, while not entirely remarkable, was appropriately violent, moved along briskly, and did what it could. On the other hand, the setting of said story was in no way, shape, and/or form relevant to these times. Worse, the design of the monster was extremely underwhelming. If you want to see a werewolf film on the big screen, this is your only option besides (shudder) "Twilight: New Moon"?hardly any choice if you ask me. But if you want to see a great werewolf flick, stay in and watch "An American Werewolf in London", "The Howling", "Wolf", or "Dog Soldiers". Now back to the beginning, with pedestrian: (The + Wolf + Man) ? my $8.50) / 102 minutes of my time = pedestrian.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Chaney Can Rest Easy</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I caught THE WOLFMAN opening night (with a mostly packed house), and it's safe to say that Lon Chaney, Jr. can sleep well in the knowledge that he's still at the top of the wolf pack.To quote my 25-year old son (who also attended), "Seventy years, and THAT'S what they've got!?"This picture only goes about 102 minutes, so it's not like Peter Jackson's drawn-out KING KONG remake, but it does seem like these guys take liberties with Curt Siodmak's original script for the sake of, well, taking liberties.As everyone knows, we're set now in 1891, and Lawrence Talbot (Benecio del Toro) is an actor touring London (after living in America with an aunt) who is contacted by Gwen when his brother Ben (her fiancé) goes missing (Lawrence had left England after witnessing his mother's 'suicide' and spending a year in an asylum to deal with it).Of course, we know Ben's dead, since he's been ripped up by a werewolf in the film's opening sequence.When Lawrence gets to the run-down Talbot Hall, he's greeted by Anthony Hopkins as Sir John (who looks like Orson Welles on Slimfast). S'John is not the intellectual rationalist here that Claude Rains portrayed in the original; rather, he's a cross between Hopkins' Van Helsing in BRAM STOKER'S DARACULA and Jack Nicholson in, well, anything. Ben's torn up body has been found in the meantime, and Lawrence is determined to find the culprit.This leads him to a traveling gypsy camp, where he's attacked during a way-over-the-top werewolf rampage (no Bela here, and Maleva is limited to three brief scenes). It takes him the better part of a month to heal up (with Gwen tending to him), and then he begins transforming himself in amped-up sequences that borrow from FRANKENSTEIN MEETS THE WOLF MAN, HOUSE OF FRANKENSTEIN, AN American WEREWOLF IN London, and CURSE OF THE WEREWOLF.The inspector from Scotland Yard (Hugo Weaving)--who tried to solve the Ripper murders--is dispatched to the village (called Blackmoor here), and zeroes in on Lawrence almost from the get-go. Caught after his first night of killing (some good, over-the-top stuff), Lawrence is sent to an insane asylum in London, where he escapes, and, absurdly Kong-like, reeks havoc on the city before heading back to Blackmoor.In what I found to be a really stupid plot twist, Sir John turns out to be a werewolf himself (he not only killed his son Ben because Ben planned to run off with Gwen--and Sir John wants Gwen--but he killed his wife as well, and then bit Lawrence in the gypsy camp for good measure). When the struggle between father and son becomes physical, both turn into werewolves (sorta like WEREWOLF OF London), and Lawrence/the Wolfman tosses the old boy into the fireplace where he ignites like a July 4th bonfire before being decapitated by his son's swinging paw.At this point, the inspector and Gwen arrive. The inspector is bitten--but lives!--when the Wolfman decides to chase Gwen into the forest. Gwen, armed with a pistol and silver bullets (thanks to Maleva's advice), at first tries to plead with him, but ultimately has to shoot him. Before dying, he transforms and thanks her (unnecessary, if you ask me--we get it), then expires--just in time for the inspector to feel his wound and look at the full moon. Ah, the joys of a possible sequel....On the plus side, the visuals in THE WOLFMAN are consistently good--it looks like Tim Burton's SLEEPY HOLLOW (the same designer worked both films). The score is by Danny Elfman, further giving off the Burton vibe. Rick Baker did the Wolfman make-up, and it's mostly effective. There are CG scenes where the Wolfman lopes quickly on all fours, which look absurd to me, but the critter in general is believable. The Hopkins werewolf kinda looks like Hopkins--an interesting touch, though again, I think the two-werewolf approach is nuts.The transformation scenes are CG, and they're not very realistic. Any werewolf transformation in UNDERWORLD is better. There are some flashback scenes to when Hopkins was bitten by some 'wolf boy' that would look more at home in AVATAR than here, but they don't last long.The werewolf attacks are very bloody and gory--heads ripped off, intestines spilled, splashes of blood--but the camera doesn't linger on them. You very much get the idea, though.The performances are good. Del Toro and Hopkins are playing characters very different from Chaney and Rains, and if you're familiar with the original, it's hard to get your head wrapped around that at first. But I think they're reasonably effective. Emily Blunt plays Gwen well, even when she has to do the "fired by the hand of one who loves him enough to understand" routine. Only Weaving overplays his part in my view. One can only hope any WOLFMAN sequel won't follow him.Is it scary? Not really. They rely mostly on 'jump' scenes here, and I guess they're hoping the gore will provide shocks. On the other hand, it's fun to see the Wolfman featured seriously again.Overall, I'd give it a 7 out of 10--probably a star more than non-Wolf Man fans would give it. I'm glad I saw it, but I won't drop another $10 to see it again in a theater. My biggest problem with the film is the story. I understand the DVD will offer a director's cut (the production was apparently troubled), so I'll likely be picking that up when it becomes available.</fullReview>

My utmost favourite werewolf movie of all time was the original wolfman and its sequels, and when I heard they were remaking it I was extremely excited. When all was said and done I was left feeling a bit stale but the movie wasn't without its good moments but it really feel sort of what it should have been.After being informed of his brothers death by a savage beast stage actor Lawrence Talbot returns to england to reunite with his father who he has lost touch with for a number of years. upon arriving in england Lawrence finds his brothers fiancé Gwen Conliffe unfinished with the murder of her husband. Lawrence agrees to do what ever he can to help solve the mystery of the mysterious death, after delving deep into the investigation Lawrence comes across a Gypsy camp and gets savagely attacked by a wolf like Creator.....terror follows.Let me start off with the storyline. I found many aspects good such as how they added a bad relationship between Lawrence and his father, I found they just spent to long explaining what happened and really dragged it on a bit to long. I found the movie was slow moving with not enough action and it was made to much a love story than a werewolf movie. I was glad they kept all the same characters and added some interesting new ones, they were all very well thought and designed out, it was good they kept mostly the same background and plot points of the original. I was 50/50 on the time period they choose to base the setting around, I found it worked just enough to get by and left feeling that if they made it slightly more modern it would have worked a lot better. I was really impressed by the big twist it was a huge change from the original and I didn't see it coming.The only thing I really have no complaint about was the acting; it was top notch from beginning to end. Benicio Del Toro was the perfect fit to take on the role that Lon Chaney Jr. made famous, he was tremendous and gave an excellent performance. Anthony Hopkins as Sir John was also great as most of Hopkins's work is. I really enjoyed the Abberline character portrayed by Hugo Weaving he did a wonderful job of being an irritating investigator and being a good tough guy who has no fears, one of the best performances of the movie. Emily Blunt was a good choice for Gwen Conliffe did a great job. Last but not least an A+ for Gene Simmons and David Lee Roth for the great werewolf howls.The special effects and make up were for the most part well done, I have no problems with the make up, wolfman was perfect thanks to the one and only Rick Baker. The special effects were okay, I found at points it was easy to tell it was fake but overall not a bad job. Props were good and sets were well thought out. The biggest problem I had with the effects was when the wolfman would switch from walking on two legs to all fours it just really doesn't look right.Overall not a bad remake or bad movie for that matter, biggest flaws exist in the storyline could have really used less love and back stories and used a lot more wolfman. One things for sure it's definitely worth a watch and even though I wasn't as impressed as I was hoping I have still watched it more than a couple of times. I will always love werewolf movies and I look forward to the next one. So make sure you at least give this movie a try.Overall Rating: ****** out of ********** *** out of *****</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>A Worthy Remake</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Before I get into the review itself, I'd like to say that like many, I'm surprised by the number of negative reviews. It seems like this is happening more and more as IMDb is fractured between the huge 'Echo Boom' generation with no attention span for movies that don't have non-stop video game like action, and the smaller (but also Internet active) Generation X reviewers.Also, my review is of the *unrated* version - perhaps that accounts for some of the difference. I do not know how that version differs from the theatrical release, so please bear that in mind.Now to the review - I for one see this as a very worthy sequel to the original Wolfman (which is one of the best of the Universal monsters series).It features great performances all around - and particularly by Anthony Hopkins as the elder Talbot. And while Benecio Del Toro is a curious casting choice, and certainly a far cry from Lon Chaney Jr., he's not bad either, once you see how the character has been interpreted here.In fact the only performance that I think the original had over this one was the Gypsy cred of Maria Ouspenskaya as Maleva, played much more flatly by a miscast Geraldine Chaplin.The story is similar to the original, but differs enough to entertain even those who've seen the 1941 version a multitude of times. Also, like the original, it is dripping in moody atmosphere. From the moors of rural Britain to the heart of Victorian London, the attention to detail here is incredible, including references to Gévaudan, 'lunatics', Jack-the-Ripper, Scotland Yard, lycanthropy, etc.It also features some of the best werewolf transformations in recent film history.Ultimately, though, the film makes it's stand on exploring the beast inside man issue and the acting, facade we all hold up to present ourselves as civilized rather than a step above the animal. Sir John Talbot is a big game hunter, as cruel and vicious in his own way as the animals he hunts, and Lawrence Talbot is an actor, very good at hiding the emotions he harbors inside.This is not just your blood-and-guts werewolf film (all though it has plenty of that - especially in the deleted scenes, which hopefully one day will be added back in), it is cerebral, and akin to the classic horror films of the black and white era - but with enough modern sophistication and luridness to balance it out.Well worth your time!</fullReview>
	</review>

The film starts bleakly and it takes time to adjust to how dark the film. A son has been killed and the other brother has come to visit the father in the country mansion. It is fair to say that the story isn't entirely strong or engaging. In fact it takes time for the action to begin but when it does, the film finds itself and becomes enjoyable to watch.While Benicio's casting decision is odd and his accent isn't perfect, he does a decent job. It is Anthony Hopkins and Hugo Weaving who give the film its energy whenever they are on screen. Emily Blunt while not terrible, suffers the most from the average script and doesn't have the same screen presence as Hopkins or Weaving. Those who watch the director's cut will get to see Max Von Sydow make a brief cameo.However the real star is the Academy Award winning make-up, which is a welcome break from CGI. While CGI maybe present in some scenes, it is there only to add to the story and is not there for the sake of it. After seeing hundreds of soldiers in the last Rambo film being digitally torn apart, it was refreshing to see make-up being used instead.The Wolfman is not a film to be seen with high expectations but it is worth watching if you are in search of something entertaining.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Yes, it's flawed, but the performances, and design of the film make up for it.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Based on the classic Universal horror, The Wolfman centers on Lawrence Talbot (Played by Benicio Del Toro), who has returned to London after many years of living, and performing in Shakespearean plays in America, due to the news of a mysterious creature's fatal attack on his brother, to which his father (Played by Anthony Hopkins) seems unreactive towards. He tries to get to the bottom of the case, but is bitten by a werewolf, and now bears the mark, meaning that he shall become a hideous wolfman on a full moon's night.The film is directed by Joe Johnston, who's known for visually impressive, but conceptually thin movies such as Jumanji and Jurassic Park III. The Wolfman is more of the same, but actually tries it's hardest to provide a good story, the key word being tries. Character and story arcs are shaky, and occasionally sporadic, but much of the film's cast are impressive, and there's enough gory action to balance things out.As far as visually impressive, I really do mean it. The film is shot broodingly by Shelly Johnson, who will sometimes stylize shots with dim candles, and the creepy forests, mansions, and villages are excellently designed by Rick Heinrichs, but the biggest praise I have for the film is the artistry by makeup effects LEGEND Rick Baker.Aside from the awesome skin, fur, claws, fangs, and drool of the title creature, you've also got goatees, lovely hair styles, and bloody and exhausted faces. It's pure genius, and it's great to see Baker back and doing what he does best. He even gets a short cameo... and I mean short.The Wolfman shouldn't be mistaken for a good movie, but it can be acknowledged to be a very enjoyable movie.I give it **1/2 out of ****</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>I don't like horror films but this one was fantastic!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I don't like horror films, plain and simple. Vampires, werewolves, mummies, forgotten creatures from the depths, none of them do a thing for me, especially when they try to modernize medieval myths and add scientific explanations for the magical origins of such creatures. Fortunately for The Wolfman it makes no attempt to modernize lycanthropes, instead it faithfully depicts the story of the werewolf in a gorgeous late 19th century England. Full of period detail and wonderfully acted by Benico Del Toro, Anthony Hopkins, and Hugo Weaving, the movie really comes to life as a tragic tale of a family that has been cursed. The werewolf is brutal, savage, and frighteningly powerful and his rampages are indeed gory but they are not gratuitous. The CG is good but not great, but the effective use of music, cinematography, and outstanding acting make up for it. The Wolfman is a beautifully created period piece that far outshines the typical Hollywood tripe from this genre.</fullReview>

		<username>Dan Franzen (dfranzen70)</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Flat, nothing new</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>A year or so ago, when I learned that Benicio del Toro was going to star in a remake of The Wolf Man, I anticipated an excellent period piece dripping with horror and suspense. But the remake, from the guy who directed one of the non-Spielberg Jurassic Parks, is so straightforward as to lose most of its menace. It's a flat, almost expressionless rendition of a classic movie monster, and it's a darn shame. Del Toro plays Lawrence Talbot, an American returning to his ancestral home in Blackmoor, England, where he finds that the old mansion looks like it was bombed and is inhabited by just his father (Anthony Hopkins) and a few servants. His brother has been brutally murdered, leaving a widow (Emily Blunt) who implores Lawrence to look into matters more closely, as everyone in the village seems to think a beast or a bear or something did it.The movie follows the legendary 1941 movie's script fairly closely, and that may be the problem: there's just not a lot new about this version. The atmosphere's the same, the scenes are virtually the same, and so it lacks that sense of newness that original movies have. Now, of course, it's a remake, so it shouldn't be a reinvention of the story, but it might have been better served as a slight reimagining. Instead, what we get is largely familiar territory, so much so that Andrew Kevin Walker's screenplay explicitly includes Jack the Ripper as part of the Wolf Man's universe; Talbot and his family are investigated by none other than Inspector Abbeline, well played here by Hugo Weaving and played in the Ripper film From Hell by Johnny Depp. Abberline's appearance, rather than that of a generic inspector type, is an attempt to add even more to the spooky turn-of-the-century-Britain atmosphere, and it just feels like a stretch instead of an enriching detail.The entire cast is effective, particularly Blunt as the only one who can (predictably) save Lawrence Talbot from himself and his family's curse. Blunt's work here is better than you might expect for the role. She's pretty and has a strong screen presence, which is more than you can say for your average ingénue. Del Toro, too, is much better than you'd expect him to be, as well; he's always been a pretty good actor, certainly (Traffic, The Usual Suspects), but he's more evocative here than usual. And Anthony Hopkins is Anthony Hopkins, although he does occasionally lapse into uncharacteristic hamminess.Here are two caveats. For one thing, Del Toro doesn't become the monster until well, well into the film. You don't get to see any monster until the movie's well underway, and what's the point of that? It's not terribly scary without there being a monster. Secondly, and this is weird, but the makeup for Wolfie himself looks a little artificial. That's weird because it was concocted by none other than the well regarded Rick Baker. It's 2010, and the effect of man changing into beast looked straight out of An American Werewolf in London.Bottom line: The Wolfman is not a bad film; it's just not particularly memorable, more of a footnote to otherwise well-remembered careers, except for the director's.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<username>sascha-17</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Not truly great, but surely one of the best werewolf-films</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I didn't expect much of this one, but I was pleasantly surprised by "The Wolfman".First of all, I was delighted that they produced it as a period piece. Classic horror-tales (and what could be more classic than a werewolf-movie?) seem to work best when set in Victorian England. The dialog, mannerisms and gorgeous production design are spot on - as is the casting. Del Torro's unusual looks serve him well here - even when he's not in a horror-film he always has something of an animal about him.I was also thrilled to see that they didn't try to make this show into a PG-13-affair. If you like your horror-flicks juicy, you'll probably love The Wolfman. If only some of the other classic 30s-monsters (The Mummy, Dracula, Frankenstein) had gotten a similar treatment for their reboots. Speaking of which: It's also a good thing that this movie takes itself seriously. Unlike "The Mummy", which never dared to be a true horror-movie and wound up as weird adventure-flick with bad dialog, tacky "humor" and a disappointing creature, The Wolfman never winks at the audience or shies away from the gruesome nature of its subject. After all: Getting attacked by a werewolf should result in a bloody mess."The Wolfman" isn't without flaws though: Strangely enough, the story lacks the ultimate thrill that I'd expect from an old fashioned horror-film. There are some attempts to lure the audience into false security and then shock them out of their seats, but those announce themselves well in advance and thus never quite work. Especially the nightmare-sequences were done much better and more effective (read: scarier) in "An American Werewolf in London". The creature itself looks great and the transformation scenes are pretty solid as well. However: The full werewolf running over rooftops looks too obviously like a CGI-sequence. Other CGI-effects are a mixed bag as well: There's a bear and a deer who both look about as real as the gopher and monkeys in Indiana Jones 4.So in short: While I still prefer other werewolf-films (An American Werewolf in London, Wolfen), this one's far from being a stinker. It's way better than recent attempts at the subject such as "Twilight" (*shudder*) or those dreadful "Underworld"-flicks. If you like your horror classic, your setting and characters dark and your monster-attacks gory, you should check it out.</fullReview>
	</review>

Emily Blunt turns in fairly decent performance as Ben's widow Gwen who begins to grow feelings for Laurence. I haven't seen Blunt in a lot of movies except for Sunshine Cleaning and Dan In Real Life but she's believable as Gwen who's desperately looking for the truth behind Ben's brutal death and asks Laurence for help. Hugo Weaving is good as Inspector Abberline in his limited screen time who like Hopkins doesn't have a lot to do. Weaving however makes the best of the role he does have in the film. I think one of his most memorable characters will always be as the villain Agent Smith from The Matrix movies but he still turns in a solid performance as Abberline who is on the hunt for the "killer" responsible for slaughtering villagers at night during a full moon. The rest of the cast are okay but no one stands out. The action sequences mostly involving the wolf-man brutally killing people are well executed which is sure to please horror junkies who enjoy watching characters getting their limbs, heads, and other body parts torn apart by a creature that mauls any poor soul caught in it's path. The pacing of the film was fine and this is certainly one of the better remakes that are being re-imagined these days. I'd rather watch this than Friday the 13th, Halloween, or The Omen to name a few. I liked how we only get glimpses of the wolf-man for the first half before Johnston gives us a full view of the creature. I thought it was more effective that way instead of showing audiences the wolf-man up close within the first five minutes and the Gothic mood was fitting for the film. The location they chose was also quite good. It added a lot to the movie and the atmosphere created some suspenseful moments when the wolf-man was on the loose. The movie itself and some scenes felt a little rushed even with a running time of 103 minutes. I haven't watched the directors cut yet. Some predictable moments did weigh down the film somewhat. There's also a scene near the end that seemed a bit anti-climatic and it's kind of over before you could really enjoy the fight but I guess it worked for the film. Overall The Wolfman is a decent remake that will probably please horror junkies who enjoy movies of this genre. So if you're one of these people, chances are you'll enjoy The Wolfman. It has just enough action, blood, guts, gore, drama, and solid performances from the cast (Del Toro, Hopkins, Blunt, and Weaving) who make this remake worth watching at least once.</fullReview>

		<username>Max_cinefilo89</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>"Even a man who is pure in heart..."</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>On paper, the new version of Universal's classic monster film The Wolf Man was always a more solid project compared to remakes of Japanese shockers (The Ring etc.) or Michael Bay-produced "reboots" of Friday 13th and other gory franchises, and the reason is the makers - primarily star and co-producer Benicio Del Toro - didn't make it for financial reasons, but because of a genuine passion for the material (the original, starring Lon Chaney Jr., is said to be Del Toro's favorite movie). Then came the usual reports of a troubled production, with first-choice director Mark Romanek leaving and being replaced at the last minute with the not always brilliant Joe Johnston (his last effects-heavy work was the disappointing Jurassic Park III). It is quite surprising, then, that the finished film is more of a labor of love than a studio-imposed compromise: flawed, no doubt, but gritty and exciting all the way through.With no literary sources demanding fidelity (the excuse for the latest major re-imaginings of Dracula and Frankenstein), the script by Andrew Kevin Walker and David Self sticks to the 1940s blueprint with some minor modifications: Lawrence Talbot (Del Toro) returns home after a protracted absence to find out the truth about his brother's death. He is greeted quite coldly by his eccentric father (Anthony Hopkins), whereas his brother's fiancée Gwen (Emily Blunt) gets worryingly close to him. The one night, while visiting a gypsy camp, Lawrence is attacked by an unknown beast, and soon finds himself bearing the mark of an ageless curse, which manifests itself with the full moon.Like its predecessors (Coppola's Dracula and Branagh's Frankenstein), the new Wolfman puts a lot of effort in its atmosphere, and it pays off with a superb recreation of Victorian England, eerie cinematography and a chilling score by Danny Elfman. It also shares the aforementioned films' sense of postmodern playfulness by having one character (Inspector Abberline, played by Hugo Weaving) come from another macabre story (the very real case of Jack the Ripper) and providing its own spin on the joke of Talbot's father being played by another cinematic monster (Claude "The Invisible Man" Rains back in the day, Hannibal Lecter in 2010). However, the attempt to modernize the film also leads to its one major weakness, namely a third act that becomes a conventional action movie, rather than the riveting character piece it was up to that point.Fortunately, the strength of the characterizations outweighs the occasionally thin plotting, thanks to excellent performances: the brooding Del Toro, the worried Blunt, the bizarre Weaving and the raving mad Hopkins (always a pleasure to watch) form a flawless ensemble, supported by Geraldine Chaplin's nostalgic cameo as a gypsy ( the famous poem and cane are also present). Naturally, the film's weight rests largely on Del Toro's shoulders, and he delivers both as the tormented Talbot and as the fierce werewolf, an astounding creation thanks to Rick Baker's CGI-free make-up work. Whereas other remakes just aim for meaningless scares, this one works because the people involved always try to get it right, and it is to Johnston's credit that he didn't go for a PG-13 rating: the scary bits are really scary, courtesy of a savvy mix of suspense and bloodletting, with the occasional touch of dark humor (John Landis, anyone?).Good acting, a remarkably decent script and a genuinely frightening monster: that's what The Wolfman has going for it. Sure, the original is better, but that was a given from the very beginning, wasn't it?</fullReview>
	</review>
