		<username>rparham</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>"Wolfman" is a fun B-movie romp</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>There was a time when Hollywood studios made, perhaps not proudly, but unabashedly, movies that they were not afraid to refer to as B-movies. They were not the cream of the crop, didn't star the biggest names, were made for lower budgets than the crown jewels, and generally featured somewhat, well, pulpier subject matter. Some time ago, Hollywood seemed to stop intentionally making B-movies, largely perhaps due to rising budgets that prevent them from identifying movies as anything but a major production, however, Universal's The Wolfman proves that B-movies are alive, at least in spirit, in the modern film world, albeit with huge budgets, name actors and elaborate production values, but where it really counts, the final amalgamation of all the pieces, The Wolfman is a B-movie through and through, and a gloriously entertaining one at that.The Wolfman is a remake of one of Universal's staple horror films from the '30s, centering around Lawrence Talbot (Benicio del Toro), a traveling actor who is asked to visit home by his brother's wife to be, Gwen (Emily Blunt), after her fiancé has gone missing. When Lawrence arrives home at Talbot Hall on the moors of England, he discovers that his brother has been found dead and begins to investigate what happened to him. He interacts with his estranged father, Sir John (Anthony Hopkins) for the first time in years, and also runs afoul of an investigator from Scotland Yard, Francis Abberline (Hugo Weaving) who is investigating the death. When visiting a nearby Gypsy camp one night, it is attacked by a vicious creature that kills several Gypsies and injures Lawrence, who convalesces quickly and begins having strange visions. He quickly discovers that he was attacked by a werewolf, and that the bite he sustained has passed the curse of the werewolf on to him, which takes hold of him when the moon is full, transforming him into a fearsome killing beast, half-man, half-wolf. Lawrence must now try to avoid Abberline, who wants to take him in, protect Gwen, whom is developing affection for, and discover who it was that infected him.The Wolfman has been produced in the style of Gothic horror films of the past. Almost every frame is absolutely dripping with atmosphere, thanks to the work of production designer Rick Heinrichs, director of photography Shelly Johnson and composer Danny Elfman. Filled with foggy forests, elaborate English manors, overcast skies and interiors lit by candles, The Wolfman is marvelous to look at. Into this environment, The Wolfman itself is a mix of old and new techniques, makeup effects by master make-up artist Rick Baker and more modern CG animation for some scenes of The Wolfman in action. Some of the effects are a bit hokey at times, but the bulk of it works well enough to not be distracting.In addition to its good looks, The Wolfman provides a narrative that is hardly ground breaking, but features enough strong moments to be engaging. Director Joe Johnson has assembled some strong set-pieces, such as the Gypsy camp attack, Lawrence's romp across London rooftops and the fire-infused finale, to keep your attention on the screen. The character development is a bit lacking, but the performers do a good enough job inhabiting their roles to give us some attachment to the goings on. Special note on the acting front can be given to Del Toro and Hopkins, both who give strong turns in their roles, Del Toro infusing Lawrence with a brooding, tortured soul, and Hopkins makes Sir John a man who seems a bit out of touch with reality, mixing in the right hint of menace when necessary. Emily Blunt isn't given a lot to do, but still manages to equate herself well enough as Gwen, and Hugo Weaving gets some of the film's gallows humor for many of his scenes as inspector Abberline.The Wolfman doesn't pull any punches on the blood and gore front, it provides enough of it to properly earn it's R rating, but it works in context with the material. The Wolfman is a movie that isn't high art, but isn't intended to be either, it is a glorious throwback to yesteryear, when movies about werewolves were made by studios with the sole purpose of being a low-brow, meat and potatoes horror film, and The Wolman does a good job of providing modern audiences with that kind of entertaining package.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>A true classic</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Many true things have already been written by my fellow commenters, so I will only state what I feel is most important about this movie: If you are looking for the usual genre horror movie with lots of gore, some mostly half- naked, screaming girl victims and the usual ten-little-indians-plot - do not bother. This film takes the classic, literary approach to the beauty/beast story. It focuses on the tragedy of the hero/monster who falls not by his own flawed character, but by a sad and irreversible twist of fate, and re-discovers his humanity in embracing death over a life without morale. You WILL enjoy beautiful scenery, costumes and cinematography, an exclusive cast (though the acting seems a bit over-the-top at times), a fistful of iconic references (Hamlet, Oedipus, even King Kong, - am I the only one thinking of Ang Lee's Hulk?! - not to forget Sir Anthony Hopkins' own classic performances in Coppola's Dracula, Hannibal, etc.) and two hours of intelligent, maybe at times a bit too dramatic entertainment well worth your admission fee.If there is one thing to criticize : Like so many more otherwise enjoyable movies, this one also does not manage to convey true romance where it should (the story demands it) - maybe ten minutes more and a few well-written lines of dialogue would have helped.But still: If you're asking yourself why you should watch this movie in the time of Anne Rice, the Twilight saga and a variety of TV series in the creature/vampire tradition, then it is like asking yourself why you should read the original Dracula by Bram Stoker, the Hunchback of Nôtre Dame, the Phantom of the Opera or any other variation of Beauty and the Beast: Because true classics never were about the action, the special effects, the good-looking star, the hype or the costumes, but about the drama, the deep human conflict, and the timeless truth somewhere between the lines.Of this tradition, Wolfman is a worthy descendant.</fullReview>

Hopkins' performance reminded me somewhat of his turn as Professor Von Helsing in 1992's 'Dracula,' and indeed sometimes 'The Wolfman' looks and sounds like that earlier (and much better) film. Still, 'The Wolfman' is a fun ride for the most part and surprisingly faithful to the 1941 classic on which it is largely based.</fullReview>

All in all a howling good movie, a tad intelligent, full on the gore, occasional scares and a story that if you let it will suck you in.7 out of 10.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>'The Wolfman' is a welcome return to Gothic horror</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>In The Wolfman, loosely based on the 1941 horror classic of the same name, Benicio Del Toro plays Lawrence Talbot who reunites with his estranged father Sir John Talbot (Anthony Hopkins) as he returns to his ancestral home in Victorian England to find out more about his brother's mysterious murder. With help from his brother's wife, Gwen Conliffe (Emily Blunt), Lawrence finds a link to a gypsy camp where he gets bitten by a wild beast, which in turn, turns him into a werewolf. Meanwhile, Abberline (Hugo Weaving) of Scotland Yard is investigating Lawrence.The film is a visual marvel. It is amazing the amount of work that has went into the period details and the Victorian sets to give a beautifully foggy, Gothic atmosphere. Director Joe Johnston (Jurassic Park 3) seemed to have made sure there was always something going on in the background, which could've gotten in the way of creating any real tension, but for the most part, made the film more immersive. To have this much attention to detail in a monster movie since Francis Coppola's Dracula is quite refreshing.Anthony Hopkins' acting and delivery of lines give the film an extra weight that makes it feel elegant. One can't help but feel he had a lot of fun playing the role of Lawrence's father, who is a little more complicated than he first appears. Benicio Del Toro as the lead was an interesting, but welcome choice. There's something about him that's a little off, who is not immediately likable or conventional, nor "dapper" in a British sense, but he is a man with a haunted past, and his character fits perfectly within this genre. As a werewolf, he's perfect. Emily Blunt is likable as the semi-love interest, although her role is not a substantial one as it could've been in the film. Hugo Weaving playing a detective from Scotland Yard probably wasn't a huge stretch for him, but he plays his role as only he could play.So, now, how was the wolf, you ask? I thought the makeup and CG effects of the werewolf looked really cool. I really liked the way it moved and it had a great amount of menace. The transformation scene is reminiscent of American Werewolf in London, but with some improved CG. I had already dug the whole look of the background, so just watching the wolf man effortlessly running across London rooftops is quite a site to behold. I must say I had missed that "classic" look of the werewolf past and it was refreshing to see it again. There's good amount of gore as expected, but fits well with the essence of the film.This film marks a welcome return to Gothic horror, and I must say I had a lot of fun watching The Wolfman. It has pretty much everything one can expect from the monster movie genre?action, gore, shocks, amazing sets, and cool effects. Andrew Kevin Walker's (Sleepy Hollow) script, while it has a small twist, doesn't make any bold or surprising choices, but stays faithful to the genre. After all, this is The Wolfman. The film doesn't go into any deep psychoanalysis or philosophy, but what it is is a fun, Gothic horror flick, helped by great sets and visual flair. I give The Wolfman *** ? out of **** stars.More of my reviews are updated on http://twitter.com/d_art</fullReview>

		<fullReview>There were rumors of production problems with The Wolfman on the internet towards the end of last year. When critics finally got a look at the finished product, it is probably unsurprising that they generally wrote it off as a failure of a film. Of course, much madness and mayhem follows.The casting for this film was great. Del Toro looks like a wolf before you even apply any makeup and is totally believable in the title role. Hopkins plays the father of Lawrence, and although some critics have accused him of taking this role as a cash in, I disagree. Hopkins most famous role is Hannibal Lecter, a horror villain, and a film like The Wolfman is always going to be able to use his creepiness effectively. Hugo Weaving is also good as a Scotland Yard inspector the tries to stop the beast from doing more damage.Being a period piece set in the 1890s, the film has a nice, appropriately dark, vintage look to it. Once the action started it delivered the goods. In a time when we have a series like Twilight turning vampires into pathetic little wussies, its good to still have a werewolf movie that features multiple decapitations in a murderous rampage. 		<shortDescription>10% Universal Classic Monsters, 10% of An American Werewolf in London, and 80% Hammer Horror Film</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Wolfman reminds me of the sequels to the original Universal monster movies of the 30's and 40's. You like them. You watch them because you want more of your favorite monster characters. But you know that they're not completely up to par or as complex as their originals.Before I move forward, I want to give credit where credit is due. When I make reference to Hammer Films in this review, it's because Gary Murray from BigFanBoy.com pointed out to me after the screening the similarities between this remake and those classic British films. I just didn't want to "steal" anything away from what he ends up writing.As far as the setting of the film, it is most definitely a period piece. This hearkens back to the Hammer films more than the Universal ones, which had modern day settings. It also has a very heavy Gothic overtone. From foggy countrysides to the dark and dank hallways of the Talbot homestead. This really does a great job of capturing atmosphere.Storywise, it's really nothing new. It's at times comfortably familiar and at others a little thin. It feels like maybe something's missing throughout. Through the entire climax of the film, I couldn't help but keep thinking of the Jack Nicholson movie, Wolf.Effects-wise, this is a big treat for horror fans. The transformation scenes and wolf makeup are very realistic and convincing, thanks to the legendary Rick Baker working his FX magic. The CGI city and background scenery is very well put together and looks beautiful. Furthermore bringing on the Hammer comparisons, keeping the blood and gore in this film were definitely one of director Joe Johnston's main priorities. There are Technicolor red splashes of blood everywhere. Body parts fly in the air and flop on the ground almost nonstop during any scenes of action with the werewolf. Stomachs are slashed, intestines spilling out all over the ground. Heads are lopped off. This is the real deal! Another thing I found very interesting was Danny Elfman's soundtrack. He really reared it in with this one. There's no signature kooky Beetlejuice or Edward Scissorhands vibe here. This is pretty much Elfman paying homage to the classic horror films and soundtracks of the past.The actors all do relatively well in their parts. Benecio Del Toro plays Lawrence Talbot as a brooding and pained individual. Anthony Hopkins plays Talbot's father with a delicious and vindictive air that shows how much fun he must have had playing the part. Hugo Weaving is commanding in his role of the Scotland Yard agent assigned to find out who's committing the vicious murders in the countryside. Emily Blunt does a great job playing the heartbroken fiancé of Talbot's brother, who becomes sympathetic and emotionally drawn to the helplessly cursed Lawrence.Overall, what you've got with The Wolfman is an interesting mix of 10% Universal Classic Monsters, 10% of An American Werewolf in London, and 80% Hammer Horror Film. I find it strange that Universal somehow ended up mimicking and taking so many elements from the Hammer films, seeing that back in the day they would have or did keep the British studio from using any of their monster designs or images. It's ironic how the tables have turned ? the influenced became the influencers.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>The Wolfman ? Howling tribute to the classic original</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Anyone expecting something totally different to previous werewolf films should not go to see this film. However, those expecting a painstaking reverence to the 1941 original will be pleased. This remake stays extremely true to its source material from character names to the look of the Wolfman himself. Rick Baker has said in countless interviews that Jack Pierce's make-up effects in the original film are what inspired him to enter into the field. Here, he keeps much of the original look of the Wolfman, adding subtle, slightly more realistic updates to the overall design.Those simply expecting to be entertained will appreciate the fact that the film pretty much follows the original with its simple beginning-middle-end narrative approach that epitomised classic Hollywood. There is no heavy interweaving of overcomplicated subplots that characterise many modern films. In essence, the film is like the original with its narrative functioning somewhat like a Greek tragedy. Our hero is doomed by his destiny to fall to his demise due to the family curse. In this case the curse being lycanthropy.Set in 1891, Lawrence Talbot (Benicio del Torro) is an American Shakespearean actor who returns to his family estate in Blackmoor after the horrific death of his brother Ben at the hands of some wild animal. His father, Sir John Talbot (Sir Anthony Hopkins), appears cold and distant but tells Lawrence that he is glad he has returned. After identifying his brother's dismembered body Lawrence gives his brother's personal effects to Gwen Conliffe (Emily Blunt), Ben's fiancée. Lawrence is haunted by the death of his mother and has dreams concerning her death.The local villagers are superstitious and believe a monster is responsible for Ben Talbot's death, and that local gypsies may be involved.Lawrence investigates the scene of his brother's murder and stumbles upon a hunting party at the gypsy camp. A wolf attacks the camp and in the ensuing mayhem leaves behind mutilated corpses. Lawrence chases the monster and is savagely bitten during an encounter with the beast before he is rescued. This sets in motion a chain of events that uncovers the cause of his brother's death and the dark, tragic Talbot family secret.The cast is solid with performances that seamlessly flow and feed off each other. You can see the interaction between them depicting raw and realistic emotions. Benicio del Torro blends the right mixture of charm and desperate sadness to Lawrence. Sir Anthony Hopkins was an inspired choice as he once again gives us another memorable steely, malevolent villain in Sir John. Hugo weaving shows us how he is one of best character actors in the business as inspector Fredrick Aberline. The beautiful Emily Blunt rounds out the cast well with her performance as Gwen. She continues show that she is a talent to watch out for in the coming years.Danny Elfman's robust score and Shelley Johnson's melancholic cinematography suitably adds weight to the gloomy atmosphere.My only issue with The Wolfman is the CGI sequences that shows the werewolf move with a grace and speed that makes the creature appear light as a feather instead of being a weighty, powerful beast. Still, the match of Rick Baker's make-up effects and the CGI sequences still make it better than werewolf incarnations in Underworld and Van Helsing.Overall, The Wolfman deserves to be credited alongside Dog Soldiers as the best werewolf films since An American Werewolf in London.</fullReview>
	</review>

Lawrence returns to his native England upon the request of his brother's distraught fiancée Gwen Conliffe, played by Golden Globe winner Emily Blunt (Gideon's Daughter, The Devil Wears Prada). 		<shortDescription>Entertaining, Stylistic, Violent and even a little Cheezy</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Wolfman brings back Universal's classic horror-icon back now more amped up that before. At times the more psychedelic moments do seem to cross the point of unintentional hilarity but the effort is still quite good.Looking from the view-point of horror films The Wolfman is very weak on suspense. There is of course some blood and gore but due to its over-the-top nature serves more as a visceral fuel for the film's actions and not really as an element of horror.However, the werewolf effects have been achieved extremely well. The film balances nicely between actual physical human-performance and CGI use. Because the film clearly attempted to stick true to the classic image of the Wolfman, the werewolves with their hunched look may occasionally remind you of the 1970s Hulk-series, but at least they look decidedly different from most other werewolves that have been seen in recent films.Sadly, as I have not seen the original Wolfman films I can't really measure how well this remake did justice to them. The script is okay, meaning the talented cast of actors actually get to show off some *acting* ability and not just move their jaws to meaningless words like in so many other contemporary horror-films. The heavy CG use is something I'll probably never get used to and there was even some redundant usage of it in this film but generally I can see why the film-makers would want to make the film-making process a bit easier with it.The Wolfman is thankfully a thoroughly entertaining movie even though it has its few weak spots. It's not, in my view, a true horror-film by definition but more of a supernatural, romantic action-film.</fullReview>

		<username>zardoz-13</username>
		<score>0.7</score>
		<shortDescription>Above Average Reimagination Is Still Incredibly Tragic</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The remake of the vintage 1941 horror chiller "The Wolfman" is nothing like Universal Studios' earlier reboot of its "Mummy" franchise with Brendan Fraser. Humorless, heavy-handed, and often hard-to-follow, "The Wolfman" concerns characters doomed to die by the inevitable hand of fate. Whereas the new "Mummy" movies bristled with action and adventure, "The Wolfman" lacks large scale action. Despite an epic moment in London, the action here is claustrophobic, downbeat, depressing. None of the characters?even if they are sympathetic?are charismatic. The family history of the Talbots is a can of worms that yields nothing but tragedy. The ephemeral blood &amp; gore and the abrupt nature of the werewolf attacks provide welcome relief to the complicated plot that the characters have to keep us abreast of with occasional speeches about "wilderness of horrors" that the past constitutes. "Jurassic Park 3" director Joe Johnston and scenarists Andrew Kevin Walker of "Sleepy Hollow" and David Self of "Road to Perdition" have tampered enough with Curt Siodmak's original screenplay enough so that this remake qualifies as a reimagination. First, they have changed the time period. "The Wolfman" occurs in 1891 rather than the contemporary modern-day setting of the original. In the new "Wolfman," characters ride either horses or around in horse-drawn carriages, while they rode around in automobiles in the first film. Second, the relationship between the father and son has been drastically changed. The protagonist's father in the first "Wolfman" was kind, considerate and urbane, while the Anthony Hopkins' father figure in the remake emerges as obnoxious. Third, the romance between the protagonist and the heroine has also been revised. Indeed, the filmmakers do preserve some of Siodmak's ideas, but they eliminate any in-depth expository scenes that cover werewolf mythology. Anybody who knows nothing about werewolves may be lost if they don't pay attention to the dialogue.Lawrence Talbot (Benicio Del Toro of "The Hunted") receives a letter from the fiancée of his older brother, Ben (Simon Merrells of "Invisible Eyes"), that the latter has gone missing. Actually, Ben was scouring the woods around the Talbot estate when the Wolfman struck and mauled him. Talbot, a well-known stage tragedian who plays in Shakespeare plays, drops everything to visit Talbot estate. He assures Ben's fiancée, Gwen Conliffe (Emily Blunt of "Dan in Real Life") that he will get to the bottom of this tragedy. The day that he arrives at his father's estate, Sir John Talbot (Anthony Hopkins of "Hannibal"), informs him that Ben's body has been found in a ditch torn to shreds. Eventually, Gwen and Lawrence become lovers. Unfortunately, the Wolfman strikes as Lawrence one evening, but he survives the attack. Indeed, an elderly gypsy woman, Maleva (Geraldine Chaplin of "The Hawaiians"), sews up Lawrence's wounds. The following day, Lawrence recovers with no ill effects. A Scotland Yard Detective, Abberline (Hugo Weaving of "The Matrix"), visits Talbot estate and wants a physician to examine Lawrence. When the Wolfman kills again, Abberline and his men take Lawrence into custody and send him back to Lambeth Asylum in London where he was housed for a time as a child following the death of his mother. The physicians submit Lawrence to a battery of tests that include drenching him in ice-cold bathes to rid him of the thoughts that he is a man-beast. In front of a symposium of doctors, Lawrence is presented as a man of horrible delusions who dreams that he is a Wolfman when he really is not. Imagine the surprise of the gathered assembly of doctors when Lawrence changes into the Wolfman and goes on a killing rampage. After Lawrence escapes from the doctors, he makes his way back to Talbot estate when he has a showdown with the first Wolfman that killed his brother. Unless you cannot put two and two together, the surprise revelation about the first Wolfman is not much of a surprise. The duel between these two Wolfmen is the highlight of the close-quarters combat.Mind you, the original "Wolfman" was not the classic that "Dracula," "Frankenstein," "The Mummy" or "Invisible Man" was. This R-rated remake doesn't defile the previous picture, but you have to wonder about the father and son relationship. Johnston and his scribes loaded down the already depressing storyline with more baggage. The Hopkins character is suspicious from the start, and he comes as close to being the villain as this movie has a villain. The explanation for the Talbot family curse is contrived. "The Wolfman" contains its share of shiver moments. When the werewolf strikes, the man-beast is sure, swift and lethal. Clocking in at 91 minutes, "The Wolfman" never bogs down, but sometimes you feel lost in certain scenes. The scene where Inspector Abberline blows a mirror to smithereens looks idiotic. The meeting between the father and a feral Indian boy is an embarrassment. "The Wolfman" suffers from a surfeit of story with contrivance all over the place. The original Larry Talbot suffered from none of the psychological problems that assail Lawrence Talbot. If you prefer blood and gore, "The Wolfman" is exactly what you will want to see.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Moderately entertaining, but it could have been better</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I generally like the movies about werewolves, but I have never been a big fan of the original The Wolf Man (1941).I respect the great performance from Lon Chaney, Jr. as the victim of the lupine curse and I admire the fact that the film found a fresh angle for the classic subject of "tragic monster"; and besides of that, it has picturesque characters, the excellent actors Claude Rains and Bela Lugosi and a fantastic costume design.However, I found the story to be a bit redundant, with a good emotional conflict on its centre (the monster is afraid of killing, but it has to survive) diluted by an excessive slowness and melodramatic dialogs.Maybe understanding those negative elements (not to mention the reduced attention of contemporary spectators -including me-), co-screenwriters Andrew Kevin Walker and David Self decided to fill the story from the new version of The Wolfman with other classic elements from Gothic cinema, from a tenacious detective to the horrors from pre-Freudian psychiatry.That was a good idea, and the film ended up being moderately entertaining; however, I found it to be mediocre.Director Joe Johnston (ex-supervisor from Industrial Light and Magic) is accustomed to work with stories which require a good amount of special effects, but he has also shown good hand to work with unadorned drama in the excellent film October Sky.Unfortunately, The Wolfman falls short on both aspects.The performances from Benicio del Toro, Emily Blunt and Anthony Hopkins lack of passion and energy, so they feel cold.The only actor who seems to be interested in his character is Hugo Weaving.Besides, the movie has pre-fabricated scenes and trite dialogs which indicate us what the characters feel, but without enough emotional evidence in the screenplay to endorse it.I am a big fan of make-up artist Rick Baker since I saw his work in The Incredible Melting Man, and he also made extraordinary works in An American Werewolf in London, Videodrome and Gorillas in the Mist.His work is truly legendary, and I was intrigued by the potential of his collaboration with Johnston, who is a veteran in the revolution of the digital effects.However, the result was disappointing.On the one hand, I respect the fact that The Wolfman uses the special effects as a support of the story, exclusively focusing on the metamorphosis.But on the other hand, the visual work is mediocre and lacking of creativity.I suppose that Baker and Johnston wanted to make a tribute to the artist Jack Pierce; however, at this moment of 21st century, it is difficult for me to feeling amazed by the atavism of the hairy man, dressed with rags and howling to the Moon.It may not be fair to compare the objective of this film with the one from other movies, but after seeing many creative re-inventions of the werewolf in the last decades, it is difficult to feel amazed by the monster created by Baker and Johnston.In summary, I think I can give a slight recommendation to The Wolfman, because despite being mediocre, it is moderately entertaining and it is never boring.However, if you are looking for an excellent werewolf movie, I totally recommend you An American Werewolf in London, The Howling or Dog Soldiers.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Decent enough remake.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>The Wolfman is set in Victorian England during 1891 &amp; starts in London where Gwen Conliffe (Emily Blunt) meets famed actor of the day Lawrence Talbot (Benicio Del Toro) to ask for help in finding her fiancé who happens to be his brother Ben Talbot (Simon Merrells). Lawrence travels back to his childhood town of Blackmoor &amp; his ancestral home where his father Sir John Talbot (Anthony Hopkins) informs him that Ben was found murdered in a ditch, torn to pieces by some animal. Lawrence start to investigate his brother's murder, the only clue is a gold pendant that was given to Ben by some local gypsies. Lawrence speaks to them but the camp site is attacked by some vicious animal like creature that kills several people &amp; bites Lawrence on the shoulder. Lawrence's injury heals quickly &amp; he discovers that on the night of a full moon Werewolves prowl Blackmoor &amp; that he himself is now one as he is cursed to transform into a beast every full moon &amp; kill...Directed by Joe Johnston this was a lavish big budget remake of The Wolf Man (1941) which was a popular black and white horror film &amp; I think this remake keeps fairly close to original story even though I haven't seen the 1941 version in a few years, while not a brilliant film The Wolfman is by no means a bad one it's just that it's sort of stuck somewhere in the middle being neither particularly great nor particularly bad. Originally released theatrically &amp; then on DVD &amp; Blu-ray with an extended director's cut that runs some seventeen minutes longer it's this longer version I will be basing my review on (I have never seen the theatrical cut although my Blu-ray has both version). At nearly two hours The Wolfman takes a little while to get going but get past the slightly slow opening half &amp; there's enough to enjoy in The Wolfman to make it a worth a watch. The murder mystery surrounding Ben isn't focused upon that much &amp; becomes an afterthought as soon as Lawrence gets bit, also I didn't like the way Lawrence &amp; Gwen fell so deeply in love so quickly. I mean they only knew each other for a few weeks &amp; even then didn't seen particularly close until the end. The twist that Sir John is a Werewolf is a little obvious actually but at least the script tries to throw in a few surprises &amp; deviate a little from the 1941 original. Inspector Abberline was actually a real Scotland Yard detective who investigated the Jack the Ripper murders even though here his presence is fictional. While the script tries to be deep &amp; deals with love, loss &amp; conflict none of it makes that much of an impact &amp; I did sort of get to a point where I was just waiting for the next time the Wolfman showed up.The Victorian England production design is top notch, the whole look of the film is lavish &amp; little expense was spared. The CGI is admirably restrained with lots of good old fashion make-up effects, the look of the Wolfman is understandably based on the look of the Wolf Man from the 1941 original &amp; as such is a cross between a man &amp; a Wolf rather than your typical looking Werewolf. The Wolfman make-up took three hours to apply apparently &amp; an hour just to remove. There's some good gore although most of it is quick flashes, a head is ripped off, a torn in half body is seen, people are shot, people are slashed &amp; cut, someone is thrown onto spiked railings &amp; an arm gets ripped off. Having watched the director's cut on Blu-ray last night I thought the film was just too dark, almost every scene was just black shadow, sure the blacks are nice &amp; inky &amp; real but black is still just black even if it's high definition black. There seems to have been some tempering with the colour too, the film has a sepia toned look about it, again maybe in reference to the look of the black and white original.With a supposed budget of about $150,000,000 &amp; filmed in England this looks great, the production values are slick &amp; Victorian setting fits the subject matter, the superstition &amp; action wonderfully. Talbot Manor is in fact Chatsworth House in Derbyshire which isn't too far from me actually. The acting is alright but maybe not as good as it could have been considering the cast, Hopkins &amp; Del Toror are OK with Hugo Weaving putting in the best performance.The Wolfman is a good solid remake of a much loved classic (although I don't rate it that much), it does what is expected &amp; updates the effects &amp; lavishes all sorts of sumptuous production design on it. The basic story is the same &amp; the makers give it a modern lick of paint, a decent enough time waster but nothing I could call brilliant.</fullReview>

		<username>Colin George</username>
		<score>0.2</score>
		<shortDescription>"The Wolfman"</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Most two or three star reviews I write are in response to films that are stupendously bad-- not just poorly made, but also offensive to my artistic sensibilities on some level. That isn't the case with "The Wolfman," which is too dull to provoke so strong a negative response.The film suffers from a blanketing mediocrity that compounds itself with each passing scene, and I was strapped to think of a single compelling reason (outside of a half-baked feeling of critical duty) not to fall asleep. But though my eyelids grew heavy, I watched the film in its entirety and can now offer the sentiment that, while not stupendously bad, "The Wolfman" is a film that I can't think of a single good thing to say about.The opening scene serves as a telling microcosm for the greater disappointing experience. In it, a man with mutton chops and a lantern (Victorian England, duh) makes his way along a moonlit wooded path. The atmosphere is eerie only in an immediately familiar horror- shorthand sort of way. You've seen this scene before, or if not can probably imagine it, because it's the very stereotype of a werewolf attack. And worse, any incidental suspense that's built is thrown completely out the window when the creature strikes, felling the mustachioed man in a half-second blur. For the record, I'm not sure there's a less interesting visual in the vocabulary of film that could have been applied. It sets a clear precedent for the uninspired, thoughtlessly choreographed action in "Wolfman," which has every reason to be the only highlight of this Hollywood remake.Instead, the action is just as inept as the story, which shuttles its characters around as if on rails to service one unsurprising plot development after another, and even the incredible cast (Benicio Del Toro, Anthony Hopkins, Hugo Weaving) shuck lame performances under director Joe Johnston, whose principal problem is a lack of focus in the screenplay. First, Del Toro's character returns from America to investigate the mysterious murder of his brother (our friend with the mutton chops)--and really that's enough for a movie--but then he gets bitten and the focus shifts to his becoming a werewolf. And then the identity of the first werewolf is revealed, and the film becomes a three-way power struggle between the wolves and Weaving, who plays the Chief Inspector tasked with hunting them down. Toss in a completely unconvincing love story and the bottom line is that there's so much going on in "The Wolfman" that none of it feels important.In fact, so little of the film stands out that it's almost impossible not to let your eyes glaze over and embrace the coming drowsiness. The film exists apart from its audience, completely unengaging, joyless, and unworthy of anyone's time or attention. "The Wolfman" scores a two not because it does anything horribly, but because it does everything poorly. If just one or two elements had fallen into place, maybe the movie could have been salvaged as popcorn entertainment, but even on the peripheral you have Danny Elfman's melodramatic score and merely serviceable visual effects."The Wolfman" may not inspire the sort of venom that stupendously bad films do, but it's so achingly boring that you can't help but wish you felt anything at all.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<score>0.4</score>
		<shortDescription>Lowly Hour of the Wolf</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>In the remake world, subtlety seldom wins out in the face of spectacle. While an anomaly in the world of dime-a-dozen updates that nobody really asked for (consider most of the output from Platinum Dunes), this trend is especially distressing when applied to characters that have stood the test of time (no greater offender than Stephen Sommers' kitchen-sink pastiche, "Van Helsing"). With "The Wolfman," we can now let yet another ruinous remake collect dust in the annals of history while the original shines ever more brightly.The year is 1891. Stage actor (and one-time asylum inmate) Larry Talbot (Benicio Del Toro) returns to the palatial estate of his birth upon hearing that his brother has been murdered. Greeting his arrival are his father (a Razzie-worthy Anthony Hopkins), and his brother's fiancé, Gwen (the luminous Emily Blunt). Plagued by doubt and the gossip of the nearby villagers, Larry sets off to a gypsy convoy in search of answers. His investigation is cut short, however, when a vaguely-seen wolf tears through the convoy and leaves him mortally wounded on the moor. After experiencing an inexplicably fast recovery from his injury, Larry quickly finds himself transforming into a savage beast when the moon is full.It is no secret that "The Wolfman" took a difficult path to the screen, with production woes stemming from the loss of initial director Mark Romanek ("One Hour Photo"). Taking the reins was Joe Johnston, whose resume (including "The Rocketeer" and "Jurassic Park III") didn't lead me to believe he could handle a production with hard-R leanings. As such, nearly every frame of the film feels like an uncomfortable, unbalanced melding of over-the-top gore, awkward humor, half-baked romance, and some of the most jaw-droppingly awful CGI I've ever seen (I seriously doubt its transparent fakeness is a playful nod toward the surreal touches of its 1941 ancestor). But not all the blame can be placed on Johnston, who entered the game late?Andrew Kevin Walker's ("Se7en") and David Self's screenplay (which reeks of behind-the-scenes committee meddling) crams in so many distracting tangents that the plot never fully coheres.The characters are mere chess pieces being manipulated, and clumsily, at that: the initial scenes with Talbot's brother are a clunky catalyst for Larry's return; the relationship between Larry and his father is heavy-handed and distracting (especially when the laughable final "twist" is revealed), leaving Gwen (who, as portrayed by the game Blunt, is the film's saving grace) stranded in a half-baked romantic subplot (something that was key to the original's success). Later on, a Van Helsing-esquire character played by Hugo Weaving is introduced, but hardly developed?and, like nearly everybody else, spends his screen time gnawing the scenery to a shameless degree.And worst of all? Not only are the werewolf transformations lackluster, but the final realization of the beast's look is astonishingly lame. Which sums up "The Wolfman" pretty well, actually.</fullReview>
	</review>

The overall experience is like listening to a story told by someone who would really rather be doing something else. Every werewolf/Gothic horror movie cliché is present. The characters are wooden and unrelatable, the plot is unengaging, the pacing is all over the place, the scary scenes aren't scary, the romance is laughable. Hopkins, Del Toro and the rest of the cast are so obviously phoning it in; you can't single out a bad performance, but it's obvious none of the actors were particularly motivated to pull out their acting chops and give their best.However, the sad thing is that the movie is still very much outside of the "so bad it's good" category. There are no obvious flaws or plot holes you might point your finger at and laugh, and it is really hard to eloquently express why it feels so unsatisfying. After seeing it I didn't feel angry or cheated, it simply failed to engage me in any kind of palpable way. It's simply a mildly boring and instantly forgettable experience.Personally, I say skip it. You aren't really losing much, or anything for that matter. Even if you are a Hopkins/Del Toro fan.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Hopkins struggles in dopey werewolf flick</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Universal has a policy that all its main actors should be clean shaven. Unfortunately for Universal, Sir Antony Hopkins, having a keen eye for nature and knowing that wolves have a lot of hair, refused to cut his beard for his part in The Wolfman. Unfortunately for Sir Anthony the resulting film was so bad it didn't make the slightest bit of difference.Wolfman is a typical b-movie; predictable plot, hammy acting, buckets of gore and a great looking female lead. What makes it slightly different from others in its genre is the size of the budget, allegedly around $85 million. What this means is that it has Sir Antony Hopkins, Benicio Del Toro and Emily Blunt in the lead roles as opposed to ex-porn stars, the blood is painstakingly added by a supercomputer instead of a bucket and it's set in the picturesque English countryside rather than a disused warehouse.The plot is fairly standard. Benicio Del Toro plays Lawrence Talbot, an actor who returns home to his family mansion after the death of his brother, where he meets Gwen Conliffe, his brother's widower played by Blunt, and his father Sir John Talbot played by Hopkins. Lawrence investigates the murder, is attacked by a werewolf, gets turned into a werewolf, gets tortured by doctors who try to cure him from what they think is insanity (it's probably Lupus), escapes, goes on a rampage and then solves the carefully signposted mystery. All very fresh and imaginative.There is no tension in Wolfman, few surprises and apart from a several graphic disembowelments, very little even to shock. At worst it verges on boring. Blunt and Del Toro are passable in their roles, but it is Hopkins who carries the film almost singlehandedly, although even he is not up to his usual standard.With a little more character development instead of a heavy reliance on extravagant sets, props and costumes Wolfman could have been a lot better. As it stands, the fight scenes are entertaining enough to stop it being a total disaster, and combined with Hopkins' presence the film regains some credibility, but not very much.www.theworstseats.co.uk</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Ignore the naysayers - The Wolfman is a welcome return to the horror movies of yesteryear.</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Despite what the naysayers are whining on about, "The Wolfman" is a great flick, especially for those of us who have fond memories of the old school horror movies from Universal and Hammer studios eras.And I know, I know, even some of the fans of those studios are taking swipes at this film, forgetting that some of their same exact arguments were once used against the originals themselves from critics of their times.So let me be clear here - if you are expecting "The Wolfman" to be the "Gone With the Wind" of horror movies, or expect it to be the second coming in terms of the genre, you are in for a big disappointment. It's neither of those things... thank God.What it is however is a sleek, fun and sometimes scary horror film that for once is devoid of dead teenagers, guys in hockey masks, blaring rock soundtracks from bands that will be forgotten a week after the film opens and dumb humor (that usually destroys most modern horror movies).There's nothing too original here; guy gets bit by a werewolf, suffers the curse of the damned, is pursued by angry citizens, etc.It's wonderful to see a modern movie look this "old" too. The colors are muted in some scenes as to suggest black and white. Fun too is the welcome return of the werewolf staples like silver bullets, fog enshrouded woods, the full moon and of course, the howling.The movie, again despite what you are reading elsewhere from people who are obviously more into romantic comedies than horror flicks, isn't really that gory. Yes, there are some pretty intense attack scenes but you've seen worse on almost any television crime drama over the past decade. While I wouldn't take a 10 year old kid to see "The Wolfman" I wouldn't say that it really deserved the "R" rating.Del Toro is effectively brooding and is always believable as the cursed Larry Talbot. Hopkins is icy cool as his father who has his own issues to deal with.The film itself is beautiful. The cinematography, the surreal flashbacks, etc all play out within a plot that includes ample amounts of armchair psychology, amazing special effects (even the CGI - as much as I hate it - looked good) and atmosphere so thick that you can cut it with a knife (or werewolf claw).Overall - this is a fantastic and welcome return of the horrors of yesteryear. Leave your brain and your lofty expectations at the door and enjoy.= rev greg=</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Earnest old-fashion remake with (unavoidably) contemporary technology</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Interesting that scenes in this movie evoke right away images of Sherlock Holmes (not Robert Downey Jr, much as I like him, but Peter Cushing) country ? foggy, sinister moor terrains where Holmes and Watson hunt down the hideous, haunting Hound of Baskerville. This same Gothic landscape reflects also the filmmakers' earnest effort to stay faithful to the original 70-year-old classic. Indeed, Benicio Del Toro who has the titled lead, intimated: "Lon Cheney Jr is always going to be the Wolfman to me. It's about saying thank you to these movies in a way, and respecting and honouring them in a way".The plot line, quite uncomplicated, is not difficult to render to an effective narrative. Del Toro is a good casting choice, projecting Lawrence Talbot's torturing transition from a taciturn stage actor to an agonized wolfman. The movie starts with a pleading letter from his brutally murdered brother's fiancée (Emily Blunt) which brings him across the Atlantic back to desolate Blackmoor where he plunges into a fateful investigation. Undercurrents of tension at the re-encounter with his long estranged father (Anthony Hopkins) develops gradually into revelations of dark family secrets that have been continuously haunting him since his mother's horrible death 25 years ago. More violent murders bring to the scene a Scotland Yard inspector (Hugo Weaving) with the instinct and nose of a bloodhound. This basically completes the players except for the usual red herrings, such as the wandering a Gypsy band that is also Doyle's favourite (red herring) in his Sherlock Holmes stories, such as "The speckled band".When Talbot is bitten by the elusive wolfman in one of the mayhem scenes, the game is afoot (apology for the repeated references and confession for being a hard-core Holmes fan). There is very little unpredictability: if you don't know where things are going because you haven't seen the original, you would have guessed it anyway. And to guide you, there is always the axiom of superstars: if a movie has one, there will surely be something worthwhile for him to do (unless it's clearly a cameo appearance).The pleasure offered by this movie is therefore the recreation of the Gothic world of horror and identity crisis. With the actors, there is no problem. As mentioned, Oscar-proved Del Toro believably portrays the living hell that Lawrence Talbot is going through. Hopkins not only nails his character dead on, but also had a whale of a time doing it. "Fathers and sons have very complicated relationships?..This character is cold right through. I'm good at playing that and I have a hard streak", he is reported to have commented thus, probably with a twinkle of amusement in his eyes. Blunt approached her role with professional urgency even when there isn't much going for her. With the aura of Elrond and Mr. Smith, two of his signature icon roles, Weaving knows how to make his presence felt whenever he appears in a scene.The problem is not so much with the human characters, but their alter egos. I venture to suggest that 70 years ago, it would not have been very difficult to create on screen a wolfman that was really scary. But after all these years from Wookies to Logan/Wolverine, what is one to do? People scream from mirth rather than from horror at hairy monsters at large! But if the movie makers should not be overly faulted for not coming up with a version of wolfman that is visually terrifying, they do deserve some blame for relying a tad too much on the "sudden shock" scare tactics, especially big-bang noises, which some considered the laziest attempt to deliver a scare. As to blood and gore, it depends on where you stand. The movie makers have exhibited a passion for decapitation, and a general flare for severed limbs as well as mutilated torsos, both back and front. Connoisseurs should be reasonably satisfied while some others will undoubtedly consider it excessive. Both, however, must agree that they did it with a style that is a cut (no pun intended) above the run-of-the-mill horror B-movies.In the final analysis, even if the result spells dubious achievements, the movie makers deserve some credit for going that extra step in their attempt to recreate an authentic classic horror that stays apart from the usual fodder with which today's horror junkies are fed.</fullReview>

		<date>2010-02-15</date>
		<username>RICKW1</username>
		<score>1.0</score>
		<shortDescription>Absolutely Magnificent!!</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Lovers of the great Classic Movie Monsters have finally been given what we have been waiting for, for many years: a top of the line, major motion picture remake of Universal Studio's iconic " The Wolfman". This film will disappoint none of of the original film's fans and is in itself, a wonderful companion piece to the original.I saw this film at a 12:01 a.m. premiere, three days before Valentine's Day. I was fortunate to see it in a beautiful theatre with a great screen and sound system. And what a Valentine it was and is to hard core fans of the Thrillers of the '30s and '40s.There are so many great things about this film, that its' difficult to decide which direction to approach it from.If I had to condense my overall experience seeing the film watching it among what appeared to be true devotees of this genre ( it was, after all, midnight on a week night), I would say the following: The film itself begins and ends, is book-ended, between gorgeous upward shots of the full moon. From the first shot of the moon to the final shot of the moon, I felt as if the film grabbed me,lifted me off the ground, ran with me for 102 minutes, then threw me to the ground that I might catch my breath long enough to read the credits. It is truly a classic film in itself. Absolutely thrilling and completely satisfying.First and foremost are the actors bringing the film to life. I honestly hadn't noticed Bernicio Del Torro before this film and wondered why he was cast as Lawrence Talbot in the first place. Well, I was quickly educated as to why. His portrayal of a character wounded by the past and determined to confront the terrifying realities which are now thrust upon him, is deeply moving and totally convincing. In some ways he reminded me more of Oliver Reed's character Leon in "The Curse Of The Werewolf"(1961)than Lon Chaney's in "The Wolfman"(1941). Del Torro's performance rings very authentic, real, and true. He creates an interpretation of the character Lawrence Talbot that makes one think that in another universe this all could have happened to a man.Anthony Hopkins never disappoints and his performance here as John Talbot, Lawrence's father,and in several instances acting almost as narrator of events past and present, gives the film a profound sense of continuity, literacy, and logic that could have been lost given the action and pacing at which the film proceeds.And in regard to that action, hold on to your hats! I came to this film expecting to be entertained,not bowled over. The Wolfman's attacks are done in such a fabulous way, one is pulled right into the scene, and left breathless. One reviewer I read put it something like this: ...given the familiarity that most film goers will have with much of the storyline of the source material, the thrills actually come in the viewer's expectation of "I wonder how they're gonna do this"? Believe me the film dos not disappoint when it does the anticipated and thrills when it does the "not so anticipated".The rest of the cast give exceptionally nuanced and engaging performances. Emily Blunt stands out as the fiancé of Lawrence's dead brother. In her Gwen, she creates a character who is strong, moral, and in many ways beautifully stoic.Hugo Weaving's turn as the Scotland Yard Inspector Abberline balances the film's cast with a presence that is at times menacing but also humorous. Unlike so many of the recent sadistic,cruel,and just plain boring gore fests that try and pass for thrillers these days, The Wolfman's cast of characters are easy for the audience to empathize with and actually come to care about. So many of the newer torture and chop'em up flicks, feature characters who are simply amoral, crude, and lacking in any human quality that an audience can relate to or care about. The cast here invites the audience to enter into a thrilling battle of love verses the powers of darkness in a very entertaining and exciting way.Director Joe Johnston has created along with with the incredibly talented Cinematographer Shelly Johnson an exquisitely beautiful looking fantasy film. Andrew Kevin Walker and David Self have written a screen play that pays due respect and homage to the original 1941 film, while creating both a literate and fascinating take on the original source material of writer Curt Siodmak who actually created much of Hollywood's WolfMan mythology.Rick Baker's WolfMan makeup wisely creates a "Wolf Man" and not simply a scary looking wolf, which I felt was the case in the 1981 film "American Werewolf In London". He expands upon Jack Pierce's original WolfMan makeup, and the results are terrifying. He deserves an Oscar.Danny Elfman's score is both thrilling and beautiful, (I downloaded the soundtrack from ITUNES and love it).To movie goers who truly "get" the metaphors and symbolism that are at work in any of classic monster story-lines, this film will not disappoint.My hats off to Benicio Del Torro and Joe Johnston for creating such a wonderful homage and thrilling film experience to what is one of the greatest "Beast in all of us" metaphors ever written for the screen: THE WOLFMAN. A must see!!!</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Well-done 21st Century remake of the 1941 Universal horror classic</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I thought this was a very well-done remake of Universal's 1941 "The Wolf Man" (one of their last "classic" horror films with all the famous monsters we now know and love). Although yes, parts of the storyline were different from the original 1941 Lon Chaney Jr. version, I was still quite glad to see how much detail and care the 2010 Benicio Del Toro version gave to recreating (as if in direct tribute to) the overall "feel" of the original, particularly in the visual/atmospheric sense.The gore, shock, and special effects of today were there, but none were overdone and all were well within the context of the story. These shots were usually shown quickly and/or in shadowy darkness, yet still you saw enough to realize what actually was happening or had happened.Now some of the negative reviews here seem to be from people who may not be too familiar with films from the past to put "The Wolfman" in proper perspective; the "bad" acting or "understated" acting is one complaint in particular. On the contrary, the "understated" acting was not "bad" and actually served a strong purpose: to show the contrast between the calm and the following storm. Just like in "Silence of the Lambs", it was that eerie calmness of Anthony Hopkins' portrayal of Hannibal Lecter that made him even more terrifying when we finally get a glimpse into the depths of his true savage nature.As a fan of older movies, the current films I do enjoy are those that can use (not OVER-use) special effects and such that appeal to today's jaded, de-sensitized audiences, but yet stay respectfully true to the classics of old Hollywood where it all began. Perhaps I'm simply not as cynical and "cool" as are those who grew up with the gorefest-slasher, blowup-everything-in-sight, CGI-to-the-max, and sexius-gratuitous movies that have so dominated the box office today (albiet not always succe$$fully) ...and I'm proud of it. :) So my thanks and my hat's off to director Joe Johnston and the entire cast and crew ...I even liked Hugo Weaving without a mask! lol</fullReview>

		<score>0.8</score>
		<shortDescription>A must-see for horror fans</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This is a film that I had anticipated for what felt like forever. The film had been bumped from a potential release date at least three or four times and this last bump from November 2009 to it's release in February of 2010 felt longer than any of the other bumps. Not only that, but the film had a lot of problems in post-production. Announcements of two weeks of reshoots, a change in directors, the soundtrack being changed and then reverted back, a massive overhaul in The Wolfman's transformation, nearly twenty minutes of footage being removed, and several versions of the film being edited at the same time made the film seem like it was going to be a disaster. The film that was released in theaters wound up being one that is a must for any horror fan.Out of all the classic Universal monster films, The Wolfman was my least favorite. Frankenstein, Dracula, and even The Invisible Man all seemed to be in a league of their own while The Wolfman didn't seem to fit in the same demographic that those films did. Once this film went into production, the cast was announced, and pictures leaked from the set, I was extremely excited. This remake had everything going for it to be everything the original film was and then some. So that's the mindset I had going into the film and luckily it didn't disappoint. The homages to the original film are definitely there, especially during Lawrence's first transformation sequence and attack on the men hunting him. Not only that, but the film is practically overflowing with blood and gore. With films relating to vampires or werewolves, they need to be scary and intimidating. We need to know what these monsters are capable of. The carnage and bloodshed is glorious in this film. In fact, as a horror fan, it gave me chills. The film's setting in 1891 or thereabouts helped establish the mood and look of the film and was certainly used to its advantage since it's hard to imagine the film having as much of an impact in a different setting. But above all else, this movie should be seen in the theater for the sound. Hearing this type of film in surround sound is one of the reasons I keep going back to theater. There was a scene with water dripping that sounded like it was dripping right behind me and hearing that howl shake the floor and vibrate your seat is a joy. If you plan on seeing this movie, see it in a theater you know has spectacular sound. It is well worth it.There isn't much regarding flaws for the film. As a massive horror fan, this film really delivered in all aspects. During the trailers, it seemed like everyone involved was just reading off of cue cards or something. While watching the film, the performances seem to build. The two actors that stood out the most were the two main ones; Benicio Del Toro and Anthony Hopkins. Watching the film in context, the way they talk is uncomfortable since they haven't seen each other for so long and their reactions and interactions get more intense as the film advances. Del Toro's performance shines during his return visit to the asylum he was at as a child and Anthony Hopkins is truly magnificent in the second half. I found it odd that Hopkins was saying everything twice in the trailers for the film ("Terrible things, Lawrence. You've done terrible things," "Be strong, son. Be strong," etc). and he does do that quite a bit throughout the film, but his character gets a lot more interesting later on. It didn't bother me in the long run. Regarding the look of The Wolfman, nothing really presents itself that there were any changes regarding its design. He looks incredible and the mix of practical make-up effects and CGI result in one of the better werewolf designs in recent years. There are a few scenes during the transformation sequences that are noticeably CGI, but they're few and far between and are pulled off in excellent fashion for the most part.The Wolfman is easily the most satisfying horror film I've had the pleasure of seeing in quite some time. It is a must-see for horror fans as it's one of the few remakes that is better than the original film. The transformation scenes are spectacular, the carnage that's left is devastating and pure bliss to watch for fans of bloody violence, and the film's sound is truly what makes it worth seeing in theaters since it's done so well. The Wolfman comes highly recommended and is one of the best films to come out in the first few months of 2010.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Good solid remake</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>I've been waiting for this movie to come out after having read about it for the last three or four years. I'm also a huge fan of the original, and all old monster movies. I'm happy to report they didn't screw up the movie. First off, I'll cover the good points. They didn't use a lot of CGI, and the make-up effects were great. Sound effects were awesome. All the actors did a great job, as you'd expect. It felt enough like a classic while being updated, too, and I thought it was a good balance. As far as the gore and violence went, it was treated just right. Some of the scenes were implied violence, for instance, you didn't actually see the person get killed, but maybe you saw them get dragged offscreen or maybe you saw blood spraying. In that respect it kind of had a more classic feel to it. But, there were some scenes where the violence was very direct and graphic, which I think you also need to illustrate just how savage and deadly a werewolf is. I didn't feel it was gratuitous, though, although some may disagree. Now, as far as the negative points, I'm not really sure I can say these things are negative. For instance, the color palette that was used was a little washed out and bland at times. In a way it helps create the atmosphere, but in a way it doesn't maybe pull you in as much as a fuller palette might have. Not sure. Also some people might disagree with the liberties taken in the story. I thought they did some interesting things with the story, talking more about the mom and brother, which were never really dealt with in the original. But it really worked here, and I'm not faulting them for putting their own spin on it. Overall this was a good solid remake of a classic. They didn't do anything to completely screw it up, and there weren't any glaring problems or complaints here. There are little things that maybe could've been improved on, but in the end, I say thumbs up for sure.</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>Back to bite the horror fans</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>If it's a bright full moon night, it must be werewolves. Nearly 70 years after Lon Chaney Jr. terrorized Welsh villagers -- and legions of moviegoers -- The Wolfman has been resurrected.Concentrating on creating a foreboding atmosphere of horror over the cheap thrills of creature-effects, director Johnston draws us into the powerful story of a man confronted with his animal instincts.Circa 1891, a Shakespearian thespian (Del Toro, fulfilling his dream of portraying the character originated by Chaney Jr.), returns to his family estate to probe his younger brother's murder. As bad turns to worse, the bite of a werewolf causes the 'prodigal son' to contract deadly lupine properties.The suspense ratchets up along with the abundant gore. The actor strives to get rid of his curse, only to end up battling the beast in a pyromaniac but unconvincing climax.Photographed in predominantly grey-green hues, the film also boasts a haunting background music score, besides Rick Baker's superb make-up design. On the downside, a subplot involving a romance with the deceased brother's fiancée (Emily Blunt) is perfunctory. Also, the flashbacks to the siblings' childhood are heavy-handed.As the tormented titular character, Benicio Del Toro is alternately solemn and scary.Anthony Hopkins as his estranged father and Hugo Weaving as a Scotland Yard inspector belt out fairly efficient performances.Old-timer Geraldine Chaplin fetches up in the welcome cameo of a gypsy fortune-teller. Addicts of the horror genre need to seek out this remake of The Wolfman.</fullReview>
	</review>

I would also like to thank Universal for not using a completely CG werewolf. My last complaint is a few jump scares. I hate jump scares, and there are some of them very quickly.Basically, this movie is certainly enjoyable. Therefore, I would like you to give this movie a shot. I really can't see too many people hating it.8/10</fullReview>

		<shortDescription>I Like It</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>First announced around 2004, and after several delays and set-backs, Universal's updated version of "The Wolfman," is introduced to the world. I will start right off by saying that I did enjoy this new version, but I can't really review it fairly without touching briefly on the original film, and why the American public so loved the original.The tragic hero presented in the original film is lovable and garners sympathy from the audience in that he is generally a likable guy in a bad situation not of his own making. Larry Talbot doesn't want the gift/curse laid upon him, and will do anything at his disposal to make it all just go away.The overall feeling of the original work is pure atmosphere; an otherworldly atmosphere, permeated overall with the arcane. The Wolfman legend is old, but the curse itself is much older; facts which do not fail to make themselves known, without intruding on the movie, the atmosphere, or the story. There is no long, preachy, tired monologue lecturing the audience on the history of Lycanthropy, leaving its viewers to apply their own knowledge of the subject matter. Those lacking said knowledge simply enjoy a greater aura of mystique, which does not detract from the work, itself.This brings us to the new film.This Larry Talbot wins our allegiance via a different means as his predecessor. While the first Larry is likable from the start, by virtue of his own personality, this Larry has been subjected to a heinous childhood, with more to follow. We feel for him, but I cannot say we love him.Fog machines. No expense was spared in the procurement of fog machines for this work. No crevice, no crook nor cranny was exempt from dry ice insertion for the sake of atmosphere. And while it did generate atmosphere, I cannot say it added much to the overall psychological affect of the film, as it was all too obvious a ploy. We knew there were fog machines. Lots and lots of fog machines.The original was charming. The remake is gritty. The original eluded to the bloodshed. The remake revels in it. The original lacked story but contained intense atmosphere. The remake had an awesome story, but the atmosphere was 2-dimensional.That being said, for some reason, I liked the remake at least as much as the original. Perhaps it was the improved story line, or maybe Del Toro's brooding presence, or possibly Anthony Hopkins' performance puffery he uses when he is not sure the dialog can carry itself. Either these elements combined to create a modern Universal masterpiece for the ages, or was a brilliant accident.Either way, I like it. It will find its way into my Blu Ray collection.It rates an 8.45/10 from...the Fiend :.</fullReview>

Anticipation from a personal perspective was high. So why wasn't The Wolfman therefore more of a classic than it turned out to be? This is not a comment on his ability, but more his commitment to a role that needs to be dragged out of him. He just doesn't come across as one who has firstly suffered from events beyond his control and secondly, become tortured by his own actions.Anthony Hopkins does his best with his role of Sir John Talbot, but his script is embarrassing and predictable at times. The final main player is Emily Blunt who, as beautiful and talented as she undoubtedly is, also fails to move the audience, given her own set of tragic circumstances. The three main characters, as a whole fail to engage and convince us of their dreadful and legacy. You simply don't feel it coming through the screen at you.Of all the acting on show, the one bright star is Hugo Weaving as Abberline, who is not afforded the screen time his performance deserves. In all, a bit of a disappointment that never reaches its lofty expectations. The opportunity to re-tell this grand story appears to fall short in all departments, aside from the cinematography, which was the high point in a sea of unfortunate averageness.</fullReview>

		<username>gregeichelberger</username>
		<score>0.6</score>
		<shortDescription>Not a howling success, but decent nonetheless</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>Of the four main classic Universal monster films of the 1930s and '40s ("Dracula," "Frankenstien," "The Mummy" and "The Wolf Man") which have spawned remakes - not sequels - the latest effort by director Joe Johnson ("Hildago," "October Sky," "Jurassic Park III") comes in a distant second behind "Bram Stoker's Dracula," in this scribbler's humble opinion."The Wolf Man" takes the basic plot from the 1941 horror thriller and updates things a bit with Benicio Del Toro in the Lon Chaney, Jr. role and Sir Anthony Hopkins replacing the great Claude Rains. It's not exactly a fair trade, but some modern touches in cinematography and camera work do not detract from the overall effort.Adding support is the appropriately disbelieving Hugo Weaving ("Lord of the Rings," "The Matrix" series) and Emily Blunt (fresh off her starring nod in "Young Victoria"), as well as a whole gaggleful of superstitious and grimy British town-folk wary of strangers and ripe with warnings about "staying off the moors." Lawrence Talbot (Del Toro) is a Shakespearian actor - with an American accent - who comes back to his estate after his brother, Ben (Simon Merrells) is ripped apart by some kind of wild beast. No one knows for sure what did it; some speculate it's a bear owned by a local gypsy camp, others fear a lunatic murderer is on the loose, still others think it was a werewolf. Well, considering the film's title, I'll let the reader figure out who is right.It doesn't take long, however, to deduce the culprit. The mutated beast attacks the camp (seemingly killing dozens of well-armed citizens) and takes a bite out of Lawrence. A young gypsy woman expresses what all of us realize, that bad things will happen and the victim would be better off dead. Somehow, though, she is overruled by the matriarchal Maleva (Geraldine Chaplin, "Chaplin," "The Age Of Innocence"), who binds his wounds and sends him home.Nursed back to health by Ben's fiancée, Gwen (Blunt), and his father (Hopkins), everything seems to be fine, except that anyone who has seen ANY werewolf movie made since 1941 KNOWS exactly what will take place and when - during the next full moon.Sure enough, within a month, Lawrence goes through the same horrible changes as all of those other lycanthropes before him. And thanks to Rick Baker (who also supervised the makeup in the terrific "An American Werewolf in London" and has a bit part in this picture) the transformation is seamless and terrifying. The results are the same, though - dozens of slaughtered Blackwood Moor and London residents who died trying to stop the creature or poor saps who just happened to be in the wrong place at the wrong time.Captured the day after (while his father tskes, "You've done terrible things, Lawrence, VERY terrible things ..."), he is put through the basic 19th century insane asylum tortures, but - again - during the next full moon escapes and wreaks more havoc upon the suddenly unsuspecting populace.Realizing a terrible secret about his dad, and not willing to subject Gwen to possible death and dismemberment, Lawrence plots to go back home and end things.It won't be that easy, however, and the concluding battle is almost silly in its ultra violence. I also could not help but wonder just what happened to the werewolf that bit Lawrence - since no one seemed to kill that particular manimal. I mean how MANY of these things are running around Great Britain, anyway?! Using the "Bram Stoker's Dracula" and "Sleepy Hollow" approach, the atmosphere of the film is dark and darker. Thanks to the creepy cinematography of Shelly Johnson ("Sky High," "Hildago," "The Last Castle") and the well-placed low violin score of Danny Elfman, the mood is set immediately and is as relevant as the movie's action or direction.Short running time may also be a plus for those suffering from such dragged-out motion pictures as "Valentine's Day" and "Avatar." As far as acting, don't look for Academy Award nominations to be collected here, but it does have its scary and gory moments. Not as good as the original, but a valiant effort.</fullReview>
	</review>

		<shortDescription>Unsuccessful redo of the 1941 classic</shortDescription>
		<fullReview>This takes place in 1891 England. A man is attacked and killed by a wolf in a small town. His brother Lawrence Talbot (Benicio Del Toro) returns there for the funeral. His father John Talbot (Anthony Hopkins) is acting strange and he meets and falls in love with his son's fiancée (Emily Blunt). While searching for the wolf that killed his brother Lawrence is attacked and bitten. He recovers quickly but has been bitten by a werewolf and he changes during the next full moon.This production was plagued by problems. There were tons of re shoots and the release date was pushed back multiple times. It's easy to see why--this movie is really pretty bad. It's all shot in faded color (which seems to be a law with all recent horror movies). It usually helps the mood but here it just deadens everything. Del Toro (who can be good) is bad in his role. Stiff and wooden all the way through. Hopkins is even worse! He seems to not have a clue on how to play his role. Only Blunt is any good and she's stuck with the thankless good girl role. The script has been done many times before and the complications added are more silly than interesting. Plenty of money was poured into this project. The settings are nicely atmospheric with tons of fogs and creepy forests and the Talbot estate itself is huge and crumbling. But the faded color and overly familiar story really wear this down. This get a 4 for the werewolf attacks themselves. There are four of them. They're quick, VERY violent and VERY gory. The werewolf himself is very scary. Also the howl of this werewolf is among one of the scariest I've ever heard (helped by the Dolby Stereo in the theatre where you hear it all around you). But, altogether, it's dull and listless. Not worth the money.</fullReview>
